Prohibition of Meeting Caravans (Talaqqi al-Jalab)
Explains the ruling and conditions of talaqqi al-jalab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on talaqqi al-jalab and identify the condition that makes it prohibited.

Core Idea
Meeting caravans is prohibited only when it harms the city's inhabitants; otherwise it is permissible.

You may have heard that meeting caravans to buy their goods before they reach the market is always prohibited. But the Hanafi school introduces a crucial nuance: the ruling depends on whether the city's people are harmed. In this lesson, we examine the description and legal consequence of this practice, drawing from the commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is meeting incoming traders before they enter the market. The ruling varies: it is prohibited (haram) if it causes harm to the city's inhabitants; otherwise it is not disliked (makruh).
The term 'jalab' refers to goods brought for sale.
2
Locate the dalil
The prohibition is based on a hadith reported in Bukhari and Muslim: 'Do not meet the caravans.' This is a general prohibition.
The hadith is ahad, so its indication is zanni, allowing for qualification.
3
Analyze the Hanafi qualification
The Hanafi school qualifies the general prohibition by the presence of harm (darar). When harm occurs, the ruling remains haram; when no harm, the prohibition is lifted.
This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference) based on the underlying rationale.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (probably from other schools or a minority Hanafi view) hold that the act is always makruh due to deception (gharar), regardless of harm.
The Hanafi mufta bihi position is the harm-based view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The preponderant position in the Hanafi school is that the ruling depends on harm. This is based on the hadith's underlying reason (illa) and public interest.
The alternative view is considered less authoritative because it overlooks the condition of harm stated in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وعن تلقي الجلب، وبيع الحاضر للبادي). وصورة تلقي الجلب : أن الرجل من أهل المصر إذا سمع بمجيء قافلة معهم طعام، وأهل المصر في قَحْط وغلاء، فيخرج يتلقاهم، ويشتري منهم جميع طعامهم، ويدخل به المصر، ويبيعه على ما يريد من الثمن، ولو تركهم حتى دخلوا باعوا على أهل المصر متفرّقاً : توسع أهل المصر بذلك. وأما إذا كان أهل المصر لا يتضررون بذلك: فإنه لا يكره. وقال بعضهم: صورتُه : أن يتلقاهم رجل من أهل المصر، فيشتري بأرخص من سعر المصر، وهم لا يعلمون بسعر أهل المصر، فالشراء جائز في الحكم، ولكنه مكروه؛ لأنه غرهم، سواء تضرر به أهل المصر، أو لا.
He said: (And prohibition of meeting caravans, and prohibition of a city dweller selling for a Bedouin). The description of meeting caravans is: when a man from the city hears of a caravan coming with food, and the city is in drought and high prices, so he goes out to meet them and buys all their food, then enters the city and sells it at whatever price he wishes, whereas if he had left them to enter, they would have sold to the city people separately, benefiting the city. However, if the city people are not harmed by that, then it is not disliked. And some said: its description is: a man from the city meets them and buys at a price cheaper than the city price, while they are unaware of the city price; the purchase is valid in law but it is disliked because he deceived them, whether the city people are harmed or not.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage explains the prohibition of meeting caravans (talaqqi al-jalab). The main ruling is that it is prohibited (haram) when it causes harm to the city's inhabitants by reducing supply and raising prices. If there is no harm, it is not disliked. The text also mentions a different opinion from some scholars that the purchase is valid but disliked (makruh tanzihi) because of deception, regardless of harm. The Hanafi school generally adopts the first view: prohibition depends on harm to the people. The second view emphasizes the deception of the sellers who are unaware of market prices.

Key Terms
تلقي الجلب (talaqqi al-jalab): Going out to meet incoming traders before they reach the market to buy their goods, often to monopolize supply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is meeting caravans (talaqqi al-jalab) prohibited?
A) Always
B) When it harms the city's inhabitants
C) When the buyer is a city dweller
D) When the seller is a Bedouin

Key Takeaway
Meeting caravans is prohibited in Hanafi fiqh only when it causes harm to the city's inhabitants; otherwise it is not disliked.

Prohibition of a City Dweller Selling for a Bedouin
Explains two forms of the prohibited practice and their ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two interpretations of 'bay' al-hadir li al-badi' and explain the Hanafi ruling that it is makruh when it harms the city's people.

Core Idea
A city dweller may not exploit his position to harm the city's people by withholding goods – this is makruh tahrimi.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira clarifies the well-known prohibition of a city dweller selling for a Bedouin. The Hanafi school classifies this practice as makruh (disliked), especially when it leads to harm for the local population. Understanding the precise scenarios and the ruling is essential for proper market conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
The passage gives two meanings: (1) a city dweller intercepts an incoming trader and offers to act as his agent, controlling supply; (2) a city dweller withholds goods from the city during scarcity and sells only to Bedouins at a high price.
Both involve harming the city's people.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is makruh (disliked). The text explicitly says 'fa hadha makruh'.
Makruh here implies prohibition (tahrimi) due to the harm.
3
Connect the ruling to the evidence
The prohibition is based on the hadith 'La yabi' hadiru li badi' (a city dweller should not sell for a Bedouin). This is a dalil naqli.
The hadith is authentic but ahad, so the ruling is zanni.
4
Identify the condition for makruh
The makruh ruling applies when the practice causes harm to the city inhabitants, especially during drought or scarcity.
If there is no harm, it may be permissible.
5
Recognize the Hanafi nuance
The Hanafi school holds it as makruh, not haram, because the prohibition is understood as a directive to avoid harm rather than a categorical prohibition.
Other schools may have similar views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيع الحاضر للبادي فهو: أنه إذا وصل الجالب بالطعام: لقيه الحاضر، وقال له : سلّم إلى طعامك لأتوثق لك في بيعه، فيتوفر عليك ثمنه. وقيل : معنى بيع الحاضر من البادي هو : أن الرجل من أهل المصر إذا كان له طعام، أو علف، وأهل المصر في قحط، وهو لا يبيعه من أهل المصر، ولكنه يبيعه من أهل البادية بثمن غال: فهذا مكروه.
As for the sale of a city dweller for a Bedouin: it is that when the importer arrives with food, the city dweller meets him and says, 'Hand over your food to me so that I may guarantee its sale, and your profit will be assured.' And it is said that the meaning of a city dweller selling to a Bedouin is that a man from the city has food or fodder while the city is in drought, and he does not sell it to the city people, but sells it to the Bedouins at a high price: this is disliked.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage explains the prohibition of a city dweller selling for a Bedouin (or to a Bedouin). Two interpretations are given. The first: a city dweller meets an incoming trader and offers to act as an agent, thereby controlling the supply and potentially driving up prices. The second: a city dweller withholds goods from the needy city population and sells only to Bedouins at high prices. Both are considered makruh (disliked) because they harm the city's inhabitants. The prohibition is based on the hadith. The Hanafi school holds that such practices are discouraged during times of need.

Key Terms
بيع الحاضر للبادي (bay' al-hadir li al-badi): A city dweller selling on behalf of a Bedouin or selling goods to Bedouins while withholding from city people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a city dweller selling for a Bedouin according to the Hanafi school?
A) Valid and recommended
B) Makruh (disliked) especially during scarcity
C) Haram in all cases
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
A city dweller's sale for or to a Bedouin is makruh when it harms the city's inhabitants; the ruling is conditional on harm.

Ruling on Selling at Friday Adhan: Makruh but Valid
States the ruling that selling at Friday adhan is makruh but not invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling at the time of the Friday adhan according to the Hanafi school and explain why the contract remains valid despite the prohibition.

Core Idea
A sale at Friday adhan is makruh but valid—the prohibition targets the time, not the contract.

This lesson examines a brief but important ruling from the chapter on invalid sales (al-bay' al-fasid). The matn states that selling during the Friday adhan is disliked (makruh), but the contract is not invalidated. The commentary clarifies the scope of the prohibition and its legal effect. Understanding this distinction is crucial: not every prohibited act renders a contract void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is selling during the first Friday adhan after noon. The ruling is makruh (disliked), not haram or fasid.
2
Understand the Reason for Dislike
The dislike arises because engaging in trade distracts from the obligation of attending Friday prayer. The prohibition is attached to the timing.
3
Determine the Effect on the Contract
The contract is not invalidated. It remains valid: ownership transfers, and the price is due.
4
Distinguish from Other Prohibited Sales
Contrast with najsh (deception bidding) which is also mentioned; both are disliked but not void.
Optional step

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد
وعن البيع عند أذان الجمعة .
وكل ذلك يكره، ولا يفسد به العقد.
قوله: (وعن البيع عند أذان الجمعة)، يعني الأذان الأول بعد الزوال. 
قوله: (وكل ذلك يكره) : أي المذكور من قوله: ونهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن النجش... إلى هنا. 
قوله: (ولا يفسد به البيع)، حتى إنه يجب الثمن دون القيمة، ويثبت به الملك قبل القبض.
Chapter of Invalid Sales. And [the prohibition] of selling at the call to Friday prayer. And all of that is disliked, and the contract is not invalidated by it. His saying: (And from selling at the adhan of Friday), meaning the first adhan after noon. His saying: (And all of that is disliked): i.e., what was mentioned from his saying: 'And the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, forbade najsh...' up to here. His saying: (And it does not invalidate the sale), so much so that the price is obligatory instead of the value, and ownership is established before possession.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on selling at the time of the Friday adhan. It is a ruling statement from the matn, expanded by the commentator. The matn states that such a sale is makruh (disliked) but does not invalidate the contract. The commentary clarifies that the adhan referred to is the first adhan after zawal (noon). The prohibition includes the practice of najsh (deception in auction bidding) mentioned in a hadith, but the focus here is on the timing. The sale remains valid: ownership is established before possession, and the agreed price is binding, not the market value. This is because the prohibition attaches to the time, not the contract itself, making it makruh tanzihi, not batil.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fāsid): An invalid sale that is defective in its conditions or subject matter, but not completely void (batil). In this context, the chapter title covers sales that are disliked or prohibited but may still be effective upon certain conditions.
النجش (al-najsh): A prohibited practice where a person bids up the price without intending to buy, to deceive others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the hukm of a sale conducted at the time of the first Friday adhan according to the matn?
A) The sale is invalid and void.
B) The sale is valid but disliked (makruh).
C) The sale is prohibited and the contract is fasid.
D) The sale is recommended.

Key Takeaway
Selling during the Friday adhan is disliked (makruh tanzihi) but the contract is valid and binding.

Ruling on Separating Young Mahram Slaves
Expanded ruling on mahram slave separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on separating young mahram slaves and explain the application of istihsan when qiyas would forbid a sale.

Core Idea
Separating young mahram slaves is makruh; istihsan permits selling one adult when two have equal kinship.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on separating young slaves who are mahram relatives. The commentator explains the scope of the prohibition, its underlying wisdom, and the critical role of istihsan when strict analogy would forbid a sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is separating two young mahram slaves through sale or other transfer. The ruling is makruh tahrimi (disliked to the point of prohibition).
2
Understand the scope of the prohibition
The ruling applies to all forms of ownership (sale, gift, inheritance, etc.) and includes cases where one slave is adult and the other young. The prohibition is tied to blood mahram relatives, not spouses or non-mahram.
3
Analyze the case with two equally related adults
When two adult relatives have equal kinship to a young slave (e.g., both full brothers), qiyas (analogy) would forbid selling one. However, istihsan (juristic preference) permits selling one adult, preserving the care of the remaining adult.
4
Apply the ruling to multiple relatives
If one adult is closer in kinship (e.g., father vs. grandfather), the more distant may be sold. If relatives are from both mother's and father's sides, the one through mother takes her place, and separation is not allowed.
5
Note the extension to inheritance and spoils
The same ruling applies to division of inheritance and spoils: separating mahram young slaves in these contexts is equally disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن مَلَكَ مملوكين صغيرين، أحدهما ذو رَحِم مَحْرَم من الآخر : لم يُفرق بينهما . 
وكذلك إن كان أحدهما كبيراً والآخر صغيراً.
- وإنما ذكر لفظ : مَلَكَ ؛ ليتناول وجوه الملك: من الهبة، والشراء، والإرث، والوصية، وغير ذلك؛ لأن الصغير يستأنس بالصغير، والكبير يتعاهده، فكان في بيع أحدهما قطع الاستئناس، والمنع من التعاهد، وفيه ترك المرحمة على الصغار.
ثم المنع معلول بالقرابة المحرمة للنكاح، حتى لا يدخل فيه مَحرَم غير قريب، ولا قريب غيرُ مَحرم، ولا يدخل فيه الزوجان، حتى جاز التفريق بينهما.
- وكل ما يكره من التفريق في البيع فكذا يكره في القسمة في الميراث، والغنائم. 
ولو اجتمع في ملكه صغير وكبيران وكل واحد منهما ذو رحم محرم من الصغير: إن كانت قرابة أحدهما أقرب إلى الصغير من الآخر، نحو أن يكون أحدهما أباً، والآخر جداً، أو أحدهما أماً، والأخرى جدة، أو أحدهما أخاً لأب وأم والآخر أخاً لأب أو لأم: فلا بأس أن يبيع الأبعد منهما، أو يبيع الصغير مع الأقرب.
وأما إذا كانت قرابتهما إلى الصغير سواء، نحو أن يكون كلاهما أخوين لأب وأم، أو كلاهما أخوين لأب، أو كلاهما أخوين لأم، أو عمين، أو خالين: فالقياس أن لا يبيع أحدهما ؛ لأن حق كل واحد منهما سواء، وفي الاستحسان: لا بأس أن يبيع أحد الكبيرين.
- ولو كانت قرابة الكبيرين إلى الصغير من الجانبين، وقرابتهما إليه سواء، نحو أن يكون له أب وأم، أو أخ لأب، أو أخ لأم، أو خال وعم:
فالذي يُدلي بقرابة الأم : قام مقام الأم، والذي يدلي بالأب: كالأب. - وإذا كان للصغير أب وأم، واجتمعوا في ملك واحد: فليس له أن يفرق بين أحد منهم، فكذا هنا.
And whoever owns two young slaves, one of whom is a mahram relative of the other: they are not to be separated. Similarly, if one of them is an adult and the other is young. He only used the word 'owns' to include all types of ownership: gift, purchase, inheritance, bequest, etc.; because the young one finds comfort in the young one, and the adult takes care of him. So selling one of them cuts off comfort and prevents care, and that is abandoning mercy towards the young ones. Then the prohibition is caused by the marriage-prohibitive kinship, so that it does not include a non-relative who is mahram, nor a relative who is not mahram, nor does it include spouses, so separation between them is permissible. And everything that is disliked of separation in sale is likewise disliked in division of inheritance and spoils. And if there are in his ownership one young and two adults, each of whom is a mahram relative of the young: if one of them is more closely related to the young than the other, e.g., one is father and the other grandfather, or one is mother and the other grandmother, or one is full brother and the other is consanguine or uterine brother, then there is no harm in selling the more distant one, or in selling the young together with the nearer one. But if their relationship to the young is equal, e.g., both are full brothers, or both are consanguine brothers, or both are uterine brothers, or two paternal uncles, or two maternal uncles: then by analogy one should not sell one of them, because the right of each is equal; but by istihsan, there is no harm in selling one of the two adults. And if the relationship of the two adults to the young is from both sides, and their relationship to him is equal, e.g., he has a father and mother, or a full brother, or a consanguine brother, or a maternal uncle and paternal uncle: then the one who is connected through the mother takes the place of the mother, and the one connected through the father is like the father. And if a young has a father and mother and they are all owned by one person, then he may not separate any of them; likewise here.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an expanded ruling from the matn regarding the prohibition of separating young slave siblings who are mahram (unmarriageable kin). The commentator explains that the ruling applies to all forms of ownership (purchase, gift, inheritance, etc.) and the wisdom is to preserve the natural comfort and care between relatives. The prohibition is tied specifically to mahram by blood; spouses are excluded. The ruling extends to division of inheritance and spoils. The commentator then details cases with multiple adult relatives: when one is closer in kinship, the distant one may be sold; when equal in kinship, qiyas (analogy) would prohibit sale, but istihsan (juristic preference) permits selling one adult. Finally, when relatives are from both mother's and father's side, the one connected through mother takes her place, and the one through father his place, and separation is not allowed—similar to when both parents are present.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhū raḥim maḥram): A blood relative whom one is permanently forbidden to marry, such as parents, siblings, children, etc.
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): Juristic preference that allows departing from strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling. Here, it permits selling one adult when both have equal kinship to the young, contrary to qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when two adults who are equally related to a young slave (e.g., both full brothers) are owned?
A) Both must be sold together.
B) By qiyas, one cannot be sold, but by istihsan it is permissible.
C) Neither can be sold at all.
D) Only the younger adult can be sold.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school dislikes separating young mahram slaves, and in cases of equal kinship, istihsan permits selling one adult although qiyas would forbid it.

Separating Close Relatives in Sale: Validity and Prohibition
Explains prohibition of separating close relatives in sale and division, with exceptions and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on separating close relatives in sale, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and apply the principle of istihsan overriding qiyas in this context.

Core Idea
Separating close relatives in sale is sinful but the sale remains valid (Abu Hanifa), while Abu Yusuf voids it for parents.

This passage from the sharh of Mukhtasar al-Quduri explains the rulings on separating close relatives (mahram) in the context of selling slaves or other owned individuals. The prohibition is linked to the sacredness of family ties that make marriage permanently unlawful. We will explore the general rule, its application in inheritance and division of spoils, and the exceptions where separation becomes permissible. An intra-Hanafi disagreement between the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf on whether the sale is void or valid is also discussed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of the prohibition
Recognize that the prohibition is tied to mahram (marital unlawful kinship), not just any relation. Note that spouses are excluded, implying that separation is not inherently invalid.
The key term is 'قرابة محرمة للنكاح' - kinship that makes marriage unlawful.
2
Understand the scope
The same ruling applies to division of inheritance and spoils: separating close relatives in these contexts is also disliked.
The text says: 'وكذلك يكره في القسمة في الميراث والغنائم'.
3
Analyze the case of two equally related adults to a minor
When two adult relatives are equally related to a minor (e.g., both full brothers), qiyas prohibits selling either because their rights are equal. However, istihsan permits selling one of the adults, overriding qiyas.
This is a classic example of istihsan over qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Compare the majority view (sale valid, makruh) with Abu Yusuf's view that the sale is void (batil) when separating parents from their child. Note the thamarah al-khilaf.
Abu Yusuf's position is specific to parents; he agrees with the majority for siblings.
5
Apply exceptions
Separation becomes permissible if one relative commits a crime (jināyah), causes property damage, is returned due to a defect, or is made a mukatab or emancipated.
These exceptions show that the prohibition yields to other legal claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد
ثم المنع معلول بالقرابة المحرمة للنكاح، حتى لا يدخل فيه مَحرَم غير قريب، ولا قريب غيرُ مَحرم، ولا يدخل فيه الزوجان، حتى جاز التفريق بينهما.
- وكل ما يكره من التفريق في البيع فكذا يكره في القسمة في الميراث، والغنائم
ولو اجتمع في ملكه صغير وكبيران وكل واحد منهما ذو رحم محرم من الصغير: إن كانت قرابة أحدهما أقرب إلى الصغير من الآخر، نحو أن يكون أحدهما أباً، والآخر جداً، أو أحدهما أماً، والأخرى جدة، أو أحدهما أخاً لأب وأم والآخر أخاً لأب أو لأم: فلا بأس أن يبيع الأبعد منهما، أو يبيع الصغير مع الأقرب.
وأما إذا كانت قرابتهما إلى الصغير سواء، نحو أن يكون كلاهما أخوين لأب وأم، أو كلاهما أخوين لأب، أو كلاهما أخوين لأم، أو عمين، أو خالين: فالقياس أن لا يبيع أحدهما؛ لأن حق كل واحد منهما سواء، وفي الاستحسان: لا بأس أن يبيع أحد الكبيرين.
- ولو كانت قرابة الكبيرين إلى الصغير من الجانبين، وقرابتهما إليه سواء، نحو أن يكون له أب وأم، أو أخ لأب، أو أخ لأم، أو خال وعم: فالذي يُدلي بقرابة الأم: قام مقام الأم، والذي يدلي بالأب: كالأب.
- وإذا كان للصغير أب وأم، واجتمعوا في ملك واحد: فليس له أن يفرق بين أحد منهم، فكذا هنا.
فإن فَرَّق بينهما: كره له ذلك، وجاز البيع. وإن كانا كبيرين: فلا بأس بالتفريق بينهما.
وكذا إذا كان له عمةٌ، وخالة، أو أم أب وأم أم: لم يفرق بينه وبين أحد منهما.
قوله: (فإن فرق بينهما: كره له ذلك، وجاز البيع)، ويأثم. (فإن كانا كبيرين: فلا بأس بالتفريق بينهما).
وقال أبو يوسف: البيع باطل في الوالدين، وجائز في الأخوين.
ثم التفريق إذا كان لمعنى فيهما فلا بأس به، مثل أن يجني أحدهما جناية في بني آدم فلا بأس أن يدفع الجاني منهما، ويُمسك الآخر وإن حصل فيه التفريق.
وكذا لو استهلك واحد منهما مالاً لإنسان: فإنه يباح فيه وإن كان يؤدي إلى التفريق.
وكذا إذا اشتراهما، فوجد بأحدهما عيباً فله أن يرد المعيب خاصةً. وعند أبي يوسف يردهما جميعاً، أو يُمسكهما جميعاً، ولا يرد المعيب خاصةً.
ولا بأس أن يكاتب أحدهما أو يُعتقه على مال، أو على غير مال؛ بنفسه، لأن المكاتب، أو المعتق يصير حيث ما دار صاحبه.
Chapter of Invalid Sale. Then the prohibition is connected to marital unlawful kinship, so it does not include a non-relative mahram, nor a non-mahram relative, nor spouses, so separation between them is permissible. And whatever is disliked of separation in sale, likewise is disliked in division of inheritance and spoils. If in his ownership there is a minor and two adults, each of whom is a close relative of the minor: if the relationship of one is closer to the minor than the other, such as one being a father and the other a grandfather, or one a mother and the other a grandmother, or one a full brother and the other a paternal or maternal half-brother, then there is no harm in selling the more distant one, or selling the minor with the closer one. But if their relationship to the minor is equal, such as both being full brothers, or both paternal half-brothers, or both maternal half-brothers, or both paternal uncles, or both maternal uncles: then by qiyas he may not sell either, because the right of each is equal; and by istihsan, there is no harm in selling one of the adults. And if the relationship of the two adults to the minor is from both sides, and their relationship to him is equal, such as having a father and mother, or a paternal half-brother and maternal half-brother, or a maternal uncle and paternal uncle: then the one who is related through the mother stands in place of the mother, and the one related through the father is like the father. And if the minor has a father and mother, and they are all in one ownership, he may not separate any of them; likewise here. If he separates them, that is disliked for him, but the sale is valid. If both are adults, there is no harm in separating them. Likewise, if he has a paternal aunt and maternal aunt, or a paternal grandmother and maternal grandmother: he may not separate him from either of them. His statement: (If he separates them, that is disliked for him, and the sale is valid), and he sins. (If both are adults, there is no harm in separating them). Abu Yusuf said: The sale is void regarding parents, and valid regarding brothers. Then, if the separation is due to a reason in them, there is no harm, such as if one of them commits a crime against humans; then there is no harm in handing over the criminal and keeping the other, even if separation occurs. Likewise, if one of them consumes the property of a person: then it is permitted even if it leads to separation. Likewise, if he buys both and finds a defect in one, he may return the defective one alone. According to Abu Yusuf, he must return both or keep both, and may not return the defective one alone. And there is no harm in making a kitaba (contract of manumission) with one, or emancipating him for a consideration or without; by himself, because the mukatab or the emancipated becomes free wherever his owner goes.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Mukhtasar al-Quduri deals with the ruling on separating close relatives when selling slaves (or animals) owned together. The author begins by stating that the prohibition (karahah) of separation is based on marital prohibited kinship (mahram). This includes relations where marriage is permanently forbidden, such as parents, children, siblings, aunts, etc. Spouses are not included, so separation between them is permissible. The same ruling applies to division of inheritance and spoils: it is disliked to separate close relatives. Then specific cases are discussed: if one minor slave and two adult relatives are owned, the adult who is more distant may be sold alone; if equally related, qiyas prohibits selling any, but istihsan allows selling one. If the relatives are from both sides (e.g., father and mother), separation is not allowed. If one separates despite the prohibition, the sale is valid but the act is sinful. However, if both are adults, there is no harm. An important intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is noted: Abu Yusuf holds that the sale is void (batil) when separating parents from their child, but valid for siblings. The passage then provides exceptions: separation is allowed when one relative commits a crime or causes property damage, or if a defect is found (though Abu Yusuf again differs, requiring both to be returned). Finally, kitaba or emancipation of one relative is allowed because no separation occurs in the sense of ownership.

Key Terms
محرَم (maḥram): A person with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to kinship, fosterage, or marriage.
قرابة محرمة للنكاح (qarābah muḥarramah li al-nikāḥ): Kinship that makes marriage unlawful, such as parent-child, sibling, uncle/aunt, etc.
قسمة الميراث والغنائم (qismat al-mīrāth wa al-ghanā'im): Division of inheritance and spoils; the same ruling of karahah applies to separating close relatives in these contexts.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict qiyas based on a stronger evidence or equity.
جناية في بني آدم (jināyah fī banī Ādam): A crime against a human being (e.g., homicide or injury) that makes the slave liable.
عيب (ʿayb): A defect in a purchased slave that gives the buyer the right to return.
مكاتبة (mukātabah): A contract of manumission where the slave pays a sum in installments to gain freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the text, for which case does Abu Yusuf consider the sale batil?
A) Brothers
B) Parents and child
C) Spouses
D) Aunts and uncles

Key Takeaway
Separating close relatives in sale is makruh tahrimi but the contract is valid, with exceptions when a legal cause (crime, defect, kitaba) demands it.

The Validity and Conditions of Iqalah (Cancellation of Sale)
Definition, ruling, and conditions of iqalah with an intra-Hanafi dispute on wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of iqalah, identify its conditions, and apply the Sahibayn vs. Muhammad dispute to determine the validity of iqalah wording.

Core Idea
Iqalah cancels a sale by mutual consent for the same price; any price stipulation is void, and the wording must be either past/future or both past depending on the Hanafi authority.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on iqalah—the mutual rescission of a sale. The author clarifies that iqalah is permissible only for the original price and that any condition to change the price is void. We then explore a key intra-madhhab dispute: the Sahibayn allow one past and one future expression (analogous to marriage), while Muhammad requires both past (analogous to sale). Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly executing an iqalah and avoiding the creation of a new sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is iqalah? Is it permissible? The text states: 'Iqalah is permissible in sale for the seller and buyer for the same first price.' Identify the hukm: ja'iz (mubah).
Note it is only for sales, not marriage, divorce, or emancipation.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof given is: 'because the contract is their right, so they have the right to raise it.' This is a rational argument based on ownership of the contract.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof).
3
Trace the analogy for wording
The Sahibayn analogize iqalah to marriage (both lack bargaining, so future tense is acceptable). Muhammad analogizes it to sale (requires past tense). Identify the asl (marriage/sale) and the illa (presence/absence of bargaining).
The dispute turns on which analogy is stronger.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
State the two positions: Sahibayn: valid with past and future; Muhammad: only past. Also note that only the word 'iqalah' constitutes iqalah; other wording creates a new sale.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not with another madhhab.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text presents the Sahibayn's view first and adds a justification, suggesting a preference. However, the mufta bihi is not explicitly stated; later Hanafi works often adopt the Sahibayn's view.
In practice, many Hanafi fatwas follow the Sahibayn.

Source Text

Chapter of Iqalah. Iqalah is permissible in sale for the seller and buyer for the same first price. If he stipulates less than it or more: the condition is void, and he must return the same first price. 99 Chapter of Iqalah. Iqalah linguistically means raising; in Shariah it is an expression for raising the contract. He (the author) said: (Iqalah is permissible in sale for the seller and buyer for the same first price) because the contract is their right, so they have the right to raise it. And he specified sale because marriage, divorce, and emancipation do not accept it. It is valid with two expressions, one expressing the past and the other the future, like marriage, because bargaining does not attend it, like marriage; this is their view (the two disciples). And Muhammad said: it is only valid with two past-tense expressions, like sale. And it is only valid with the word 'iqalah'; if the seller says to the buyer: 'sell me what you bought from me for such and such' and he says 'I sold', it is a sale by consensus, and its conditions must be observed. Acceptance of iqalah is only valid in the same session, as in sale. * His saying: (If he stipulates more than it or less: the condition is void).
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on iqalah, which is the cancellation of a sale by mutual consent. The author states that iqalah is permissible in sales for the original price. The rationale is that the parties own the contract and can revoke it. However, iqalah is limited to sales; it does not apply to marriage, divorce, or emancipation. Regarding the wording, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani in one view) hold that iqalah is valid with two expressions: one in past tense and one in future tense, similar to marriage because no bargaining is involved. Muhammad (the other narration) requires both expressions to be in past tense, like a sale. The text also emphasizes that only the word 'iqalah' (or its derivatives) constitutes iqalah; any other wording creates a new sale. Acceptance must be in the same session. Finally, if a condition is stipulated for a different price, the condition is void but the iqalah remains valid with the original price.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Cancellation of a sale contract by mutual consent of buyer and seller.
المجلس (al-majlis): The session in which the contract (or iqalah) is made; acceptance must occur in the same session.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Sale
B) Marriage
C) Both sale and marriage
D) Only sale

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a permissible cancellation of a sale for the same price, with strict wording and session requirements, and a valid intra-Hanafi dispute on whether past or future tense expressions are acceptable.

Rulings of Iqalah with Intra-Madhhab Dispute on its Nature
Expounds rules of iqalah and Hanafi dispute on its nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on iqalah and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar.

Core Idea
Iqalah is faskh between parties but bai' regarding third parties according to Abu Hanifa.

The chapter on iqalah (rescission of sale) presents a basic ruling: iqalah is permissible between seller and buyer, but only for the original price. The passage then delves into an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its legal nature after possession: is it a rescission (faskh) or a new sale (bai')? This ikhtilaf has practical consequences for third-party rights such as pre-emption and warranty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
First, extract the default ruling: iqalah is permissible (ja'iz) with the same price, and any condition for a different price is void. Note the verbal requirements and session continuity.
Focus on the shurut (conditions) for a valid iqalah.
2
State the intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature after possession
After possession, there is disagreement. Abu Hanifa holds it is faskh between parties but bai' for third parties. Abu Yusuf says it is bai' entirely. Muhammad distinguishes by price. Zufar says it is faskh entirely.
Use the table in the commentary to map each imam's position.
3
Analyze the rationale for each position
Abu Hanifa's dalil: the contract belongs to the parties, so they can annul it; third parties have interests, hence treated as new sale. Abu Yusuf: after possession it resembles a new transaction. Muhammad: change in price indicates a new sale. Zufar: pure faskh.
Understand the principle behind each view.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical effect: rights of pre-emption, warranty, and sale conditions like options apply differently depending on whether iqalah is considered faskh or bai'.
For example, if it is a new sale, the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) may arise.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view, as presented first and elaborated in the text.
Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira gives precedence to Abu Hanifa.

باب الإقالة
الإقالة في اللغة هي: الرفع، وفي الشرع: عبارة عن رفع العقد. قال رحمه الله: (الإقالة جائزة في البيع للبائع والمشتري، بمثل الثمن الأول)؛ لأن العقد حقهما، فيملكان رفعه.
وخَصَّ البيعَ؛ لأن النكاح والطلاق والعتاق لا يقبلها. وتصح بلفظين يُعبَّر بأحدهما عن الماضي، والآخر عن المستقبل، مثل النكاح؛ لأنه لا يحضرها المساومة، كالنكاح، وهذا قولهما.

Chapter of Iqalah. Iqalah is permissible in sale, for the seller and buyer with the same as the first price. If they stipulate less than it, or more: then the condition is void, and he returns the like of the first price. Chapter of Iqalah. Iqalah in language is: removal, and in Sharia: an expression for removal of the contract. He (the author) said: (Iqalah is permissible in sale for the seller and buyer with the same as the first price) because the contract is their right, so they own its removal. And he specified sale because marriage, divorce, and emancipation do not accept it. And it is valid with two verbal expressions, one expressing the past and the other the future, like marriage, because bargaining is not present, like marriage, and this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Muhammad said: It is not valid except with two past-tense expressions, like sale. And it is not valid except with the word iqalah; if the seller says to the buyer: 'Sell me what I bought from you for such amount,' and he says 'I sold,' then it is a sale by consensus, and the conditions of sale are observed. And acceptance of iqalah is not valid except in the same session, as in sale. His saying: (If he stipulates more than it or less: then the condition is void). It is faskh in the right of the contracting parties, a new sale in the right of others, according to Abu Hanifa. This is when no defect has occurred; but if it becomes defective, iqalah with less than the price is permissible, and that is in exchange for the defect. It is not permissible with more than the price; if he rescinds with more than the price, then it is with the price. His saying: (It is faskh in the right of the contracting parties, a new sale in the right of others, in the statement of Abu Hanifa). In this there is detail: If it is before taking possession, it is faskh by consensus; if after taking possession, it is faskh according to Abu Hanifa. And Abu Yusuf said: It is a sale. And Muhammad said: If it is with the first price or less, it is faskh; if with more or with a different type, it is a sale. And there is no disagreement among them that it is a sale in the right of others, whether before or after taking possession. And Zufar said: It is faskh in their right and the right of others. And it is not said: How can it be faskh in their right and a sale in the right of others, while it is one contract?
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira is a commentary on the chapter of Iqalah (rescission of sale) from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The author begins by stating the basic ruling: iqalah is permissible in a sale contract, and it must be for the same price as the original sale. If the parties stipulate a different price (more or less), the stipulation is void and the original price must be returned. He then explains that iqalah linguistically means 'removal' and legally means 'removal of the contract.' The rationale is that the contract belongs to the parties, so they have the right to annul it. Iqalah is specific to sales; it does not apply to marriage, divorce, or emancipation. Regarding the verbal form, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that iqalah can be expressed with two utterances, one referring to the past and the other to the future, similar to marriage because bargaining is not needed. Muhammad al-Shaybani, in a different opinion, requires both utterances to be in the past tense like a sale. The word 'iqalah' must be used; if the seller says 'Sell me what I bought from you' and the buyer says 'I sold,' it is a new sale by consensus and must meet all conditions of sale. Acceptance must be in the same session as the offer, as in a sale. The passage then addresses the nature of iqalah. Abu Hanifa holds that iqalah is a rescission (faskh) between the parties but a new sale in relation to third parties. If the goods have no defect, iqalah is only for the original price; if they become defective, iqalah for less is allowed as compensation for the defect. The commentary provides detail: if iqalah occurs before possession, it is unanimously rescission; after possession, Abu Hanifa still considers it rescission, while Abu Yusuf considers it a new sale. Muhammad distinguishes: if for the same or less price, rescission; if for more or another type, sale. Zufar considers it rescission even regarding third parties. The passage ends by noting the apparent inconsistency but suggests it is accepted.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Removal of a sale contract by mutual consent, usually with return of the original price.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of a contract retroactively, as if it never existed.
بيع (bayʿ): A new sale contract with all its conditions and effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Iqalah is not allowed because the goods are defective.
B) Iqalah is allowed only for the original price.
C) Iqalah is allowed for less than the original price as compensation for the defect.
D) Iqalah is allowed for any price agreed upon.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi on iqalah is Abu Hanifa's view: it is rescission (faskh) between the parties but a new sale (bai') vis-à-vis third parties, with the condition that the original price must be returned unless the goods are defective.

The Dual Nature of Iqala: Faskh for Parties, Bai' for Others
Explaining the ruling and rationale for iqala's dual legal nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dual legal nature of iqala in Hanafi fiqh and apply it to distinguish between the rights of the contracting parties and third parties in scenarios such as pre-emption, invalid conditions, and post-rescission sales.

Core Idea
Iqala is a rescission for parties but a new sale for others – wording governs insiders, meaning governs outsiders.

Iqala (mutual rescission of a sale) appears straightforward – the parties simply undo their deal. But Hanafi jurisprudence gives it a split personality: it acts as a rescission (faskh) between the original parties, yet is treated as a brand‑new sale (bay') toward everyone else. Why such complexity? The answer lies in honoring both the wording of iqala (which literally means 'to lift' or 'to cancel') and its underlying meaning (an exchange by mutual consent, exactly the definition of a sale). This lesson unpacks the reasoning through a powerful analogy with hibah bi shart al‑'iwad, then walks through five concrete issues that show the practical impact of this dual nature.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem: What is iqala?
Iqala is a mutual agreement to cancel a previous sale. The key question is whether it is a pure rescission or a new sale. The Hanafi school answers: it is both, depending on the perspective.
The term iqala linguistically means 'to lift' – hence the rescission aspect.
2
Grasp the core analogy: Hibah bi shart al‑'iwad
A gift conditioned on compensation is treated as a gift between the parties (requiring possession) but as a sale for third parties (allowing pre‑emption). This proves that one contract can have dual legal effects without contradiction.
3
Identify the two principles: Lafz (wording) and Ma'na (meaning)
The wording of iqala (faskh) applies to the parties because they uttered it; the meaning (exchange of property by consent) applies to outsiders. Each is given effect without conflict.
The wording is associated with the parties; the meaning is universal.
4
Work through the five masail (issues)
Each of the five listed cases shows a different consequence of the dual nature: (1) price restoration, (2) invalid conditions, (3) resale before possession, (4) gift after iqala, (5) measurement of fungibles.
Focus on how the ruling would differ if iqala were purely a sale or purely a rescission.
5
Apply the principle to third‑party rights: Pre‑emption and Sarf
Because iqala is a new sale for outsiders, a pre‑emptor can claim shuf'ah in the iqala itself. Similarly, for currency exchange (sarf), mutual possession becomes a condition for validity.

Source Text

باب الإقالة
فنقول : لا يمتنع مثل ذلك في أصول الشرع، ألا ترى أن الهبة بشرط العوض: في حكم البيع في حق الغير، ولهذا تثبت فيها الشفعة، وهي في معنى الهبة في حق المتعاقدين ؛ باعتبار القبض فيها، كما يعتبر في الهبة، فكذا الإقالة.
ويقال : إنما جعلت فسخاً في حق المتعاقدين؛ عملاً بلفظ الإقالة؛ لأن لفظها ينبيء عن الفسخ والرفع.
وإنما جعلت بيعاً في حق غيرهما ؛ عملاً بمعنى الإقالة، لا بلفظها؛ لأنها في المعنى: مبادلة المال بالمال بالتراضي، وهذا حدا البيع، فاعتبرنا اللفظ في حق المتعاقدين، واعتبرنا المعنى في حق غيرهما ؛ عملاً بالشبهين. وإنما لم يعكس، بأن يُعتبر اللفظ في حق غيرهما، والعمل بالمعنى في حقهما ؛ لأن اللفظ قائم بالمتعاقدين، واللفظ لفظ الفسخ، فاعتبرنا جانب اللفظ في حق المتعاقدين؛ لقيام اللفظ بهما، وإذا اعتبرنا لفظ الفسخ بهما: تعين العمل بالمعنى في حق غيرهما ، لا محالة؛ للعمل بالشبهين. وفائدة قوله : فسخ في حق المتعاقدين: تظهر في خمس مسائل: إحداها: أنه يجب على البائع ردُّ الثمن الأول، وما سميا عند الإقالة بخلافه : باطل.
والثانية : أن الإقالة لا تُبطلها الشروط الفاسدة، ولو كانت بيعاً لفسدت. ثانياً :
والثالثة : إذا تقايلا، ولم يسترد المبيع من المشتري حتى باعه منه .
جاز البيع، ولو كانت بيعاً: لكان لا يجوز أن يبيعه منه قبل القبض، ولو باعه من غيره : لا يجوز؛ لأنها في حق غيرهما : بيع جديد.
ولو كان المبيع غير منقول ، كالعقار : يجوز بيعه من غير المشتري أيضاً عندهما، خلافاً لمحمد.
والرابعة: إذا وهب البائع المبيعَ من المشتري بعد الإقالة قبل القبض والاسترداد: فالهبة جائزة، وصار المبيع للمشتري بالهبة، ولا تبطل الإقالة، فلو كانت بيعاً فوهبه المشتري من البائع، فقبله البائع: ينفسخ البيع، يعني إذا وهب المشتري المبيع قبل القبض للبائع، فقبله البائع: انفسخ البيع بينهما.
والخامسة: لو كان كيلياً أو وزنياً، وقد باعه مكايلة أو موازنة، فتقايلا، واسترد البائع المبيع من غير كيل ولا وزن: صح قبضه، ولو كان بيعاً: لما صح قبضه بغير كيل ولا وزن، بل كان يلزمه إعادتهما.
الشفعة
الشفيع
وفائدة قوله : بيع في حق غيرهما : لو كان المبيع عقاراً، فسلم الشفيع في أصل العقد، ثم تقايلا، وعاد المبيع إلى ملك البائع، فطلب الشفعة في الإقالة : فله ذلك؛ لكونها بيعاً جديداً في حق غيرهما. وكذا لو كان المبيع صرفاً، فالتقابض من كلا الجانبين شرط لصحة الإقالة، فيجعل في حق الشرع: كبيع جديد.
وكذا لو وهب الرجل شيئاً، وقبضه، ولم يعوضه حتى باعه الموهوب له من آخر، ثم تقايلا ليس للواهب أن يرجع في هبته على
Chapter of Iqala (Rescission)
We say: Such a thing is not prevented in the principles of Shari'ah. Do you not see that a gift with a condition of compensation (hibah bi shart al-'iwad) is considered like a sale with respect to third parties, and thus pre-emption (shuf'ah) is established therein, while it is in the meaning of a gift with respect to the contracting parties, considering the requirement of possession (qabd) in it, just as possession is considered in a gift. Likewise, iqala is similar.
And it is said: It (iqala) was made a rescission (faskh) with respect to the contracting parties, acting upon the wording of iqala, because its wording indicates rescission and revocation.
And it was made a sale (bay') with respect to others, acting upon the meaning of iqala, not its wording, because in meaning it is an exchange of property for property by mutual consent, and this is the definition of sale. So we considered the wording with respect to the contracting parties, and we considered the meaning with respect to others, acting upon both similarities (shabahayn). And it was not reversed, i.e., that the wording be considered with respect to others and the meaning acted upon with respect to them, because the wording pertains to the contracting parties, and the wording is the wording of rescission. So we considered the aspect of wording with respect to the contracting parties due to the wording being associated with them. And when we considered the wording of rescission with respect to them, it necessarily necessitated acting upon the meaning with respect to others, to act upon both similarities.
The benefit of his statement: '[Iqala is] rescission with respect to the contracting parties' appears in five issues:
First: It is obligatory upon the seller to return the first price, and whatever they name at the time of iqala contrary to that is void.
Second: Iqala is not nullified by invalid conditions; if it were a sale, it would be nullified.
Third: If they rescind (yataqayalan) and the seller does not recover the sold item from the buyer until the buyer sells it to him, the sale is valid. If it were a sale, it would not be permissible for him to sell it to him before possession (qabd), and if he sold it to someone else, it would not be permissible, because with respect to others it is a new sale. And if the sold item is immovable, like real estate, it is also permissible to sell it to someone other than the buyer according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad.
Fourth: If the seller gifts the sold item to the buyer after iqala but before possession and recovery, the gift is valid, and the sold item becomes the property of the buyer by gift, and the iqala is not nullified. If it were a sale and the buyer gifts it to the seller and the seller accepts, the sale is rescinded. That is, if the buyer gifts the sold item to the seller before possession and the seller accepts, the sale between them is rescinded.
Fifth: If it was a thing measured by volume or weight, and he sold it by measure or weight, and they rescind, and the seller recovers the sold item without measuring or weighing, his possession is valid. If it were a sale, his possession without measuring or weighing would not be valid; rather, he would have to repeat the measurement and weighing.
Pre-emption (Shuf'ah)
The pre-emptor (Shafi')
And the benefit of his statement: 'sale with respect to others' is: If the sold item was real estate, and the pre-emptor conceded (sallama) in the original contract, then they rescind, and the sold item returns to the seller's ownership, and then he seeks pre-emption in the iqala, he has that right, because it is a new sale with respect to others. Similarly, if the sold item was currency exchange (sarf), then mutual possession (taqabud) from both sides is a condition for the validity of the iqala, and it is considered in the eyes of the Shari'ah as a new sale.
Similarly, if a man gives something as a gift, takes possession of it, and does not compensate until the donee sells it to another, then they rescind... it is not for the donor to return his gift...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that iqala (mutual rescission of a sale) has a dual legal nature: it is considered a rescission (faskh) with respect to the original contracting parties, and a new sale (bay') with respect to third parties. The commentator explains the reasoning behind this distinction by drawing an analogy to a gift with a condition of compensation (hibah bi shart al-'iwad), which is similarly treated as a sale for outsiders (to establish pre-emption) but as a gift for the parties themselves (requiring possession). The core principle is acting upon both the wording (lafz) and the meaning (ma'na) of iqala: the wording (iqala linguistically means rescission) governs the relationship between the parties, while the substantive meaning (exchange of property by mutual consent, which is the definition of sale) governs relations with outsiders. This avoids contradiction and gives effect to both aspects. The passage then lists five practical consequences (masa'il) where this distinction matters: (1) the seller must return the original price, not a new price; (2) iqala is not invalidated by invalid conditions; (3) the buyer can resell the item to the seller before taking possession (unlike a new sale where possession is required before resale); (4) the seller can gift the item back to the buyer after iqala without invalidating the rescission; (5) for fungible goods, possession after iqala is valid without re-measurement. The final part introduces the benefit of treating iqala as a new sale for third parties: if the property is real estate, the pre-emptor can claim pre-emption in the iqala itself because it is a new sale; similarly, for currency exchange (sarf), mutual possession is required. The text ends mid-sentence discussing a gift scenario.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqala): Mutual rescission of a sale by the consent of both parties.
فسخ (faskh): Cancellation or annulment of a contract; here specifically the rescission of the original sale.
بيع (bay'): Sale; a contract exchanging property for property by mutual consent.
الشبهين (al-shabahayn): The two similarities: iqala resembles rescission in wording and sale in meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the seller and buyer rescind a sale (iqala), and later a third party seeks pre-emption (shuf'ah) on the iqala itself, why is the pre-emptor entitled to claim?
A) Because iqala is considered a new sale with respect to third parties.
B) Because iqala is considered a rescission and the property returns to the seller, so pre-emption applies to the original sale.
C) Because the pre-emptor has a right to any transfer of property.
D) Because iqala is a gift, and gifts are subject to pre-emption.

Key Takeaway
Iqala in Hanafi fiqh is simultaneously a rescission between the parties and a new sale for third parties, a duality that shapes all its legal effects.

Iqalah as a New Sale for Third Parties
Explains iqalah as a new sale for third parties and its validity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that iqalah is a new sale for third parties and explain its implications for preemption (shuf'ah) and currency exchange (sarf).

Core Idea
Iqalah is a rescission between parties but a new sale for third parties, triggering rights like preemption.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira explains that iqalah (mutual rescission) is considered a new sale in respect to third parties, such as a preemptor (shafi') or the rules of currency exchange (sarf). Understanding this distinction is crucial because it determines whether rights like preemption apply. In this lesson, we will focus on the core principle and its immediate consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key legal nature of iqalah
The text states that iqalah is 'a sale in respect to other than the two parties' (بيع في حق غيرهما). Identify this statement and note that between the original parties it remains a rescission.
The phrase 'في حق غيرهما' is key.
2
Determine the ruling for preemption (shuf'ah)
The passage gives an example: if the sold item is real estate and the preemptor waived his right in the original contract, but then the parties make iqalah, the preemptor can claim preemption again because the iqalah is a new sale. Trace this reasoning.
This is the practical benefit (فائدة) mentioned.
3
Analyze the ruling for currency exchange (sarf)
The text says that if the item is currency (sarf), hand-to-hand exchange (التقابض) is a condition for the validity of iqalah, because it is considered a new sale. Identify this as another application.
In sarf, immediate possession is required by Shari'ah.
4
Contrast with other schools
Note that other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider iqalah a pure rescission (faskh), so no new rights arise. This leads to different practical outcomes.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Likewise if the sold item was currency exchange, then taking possession from both sides is a condition for the validity of iqalah, so it is considered like a new sale in Islamic law. Likewise if a person gives a gift, takes possession, and does not compensate until the donee sells it to another, then they make iqalah: the donor has no right to revoke his gift to the seller, and it is as if the seller bought it in respect to the donor. If what is not specified by the contract remains, and what is specified perishes, then there remains no contract, so there is no meaning to revoking it. If they exchange a specific item for a specific item, each of which is specified by the contract, and they take possession, then one of them is destroyed in the hands of its buyer, then they make iqalah: the iqalah is valid, and the buyer of the destroyed item must pay its value or its like if it is fungible, and hand it over to his companion, and retrieve the specific item from him. Likewise if they make iqalah while both contracted items are existing, then one of them is destroyed. His statement: (If part of the sold item is destroyed: iqalah is valid for the remainder) because the sale exists in it. If the sold item was a slave, and his hand is severed while with the buyer, and he takes the compensation for the injury (wound), then they make iqalah: he returns all the price, and takes the slave, and the seller has nothing of the compensation for the hand, and it is permissible (halal) for the buyer, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira explains key rulings on iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale) in the Hanafi school. The author begins by establishing that iqalah is considered a new sale in respect to third parties, meaning that rules that apply to a new sale (like prohibition of selling before taking possession, or preemption rights) also apply to iqalah in relations with outsiders. However, between the original parties, iqalah functions as a dissolution of the original contract. The text then presents several scenarios: first, if the seller sells the item to another after iqalah but before taking possession, it is impermissible because it is like selling before taking possession in a sale. Second, for non-moving property like real estate, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the 'sahibayn') allow selling to another even without possession, but Imam Muhammad reportedly differs. The fourth scenario deals with a gift: if after iqalah the seller gives the item as a gift to the buyer before the seller retrieves possession, the gift is valid and the buyer owns it by gift; the iqalah is not invalidated. If iqalah were a sale, a gift by the buyer to the seller would rescind the sale. The fifth scenario deals with weighed or measured goods: if the parties rescind and the seller takes back the goods without re-measuring, it is valid because it is a rescission, not a new sale. The passage then discusses the practical benefit of this distinction: for third parties like a preemptor (shafi'), iqalah triggers preemption rights because it is a new sale. Similarly, in a currency exchange (sarf), hand-to-hand exchange is required for iqalah's validity, again treating it as a new sale. The text also addresses the effect of destruction: destruction of the price does not prevent iqalah, but destruction of the sold item prevents it because the contract's subject matter is the item, not the price. If part is destroyed, iqalah can still be valid for the remainder. In a barter exchange of two specified items, if one is destroyed after possession but before iqalah, the iqalah is valid and the buyer of the destroyed item must compensate with its value or like. Finally, if a slave's hand is severed and the buyer receives the blood money, then they make iqalah, the buyer returns the full price and takes the slave, and keeps the blood money because it is compensation for injury that occurred during his ownership.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual rescission of a contract of sale by consent of both parties.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Preemption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property when it is sold to an outsider.
صرف (ṣarf): Currency exchange (e.g., exchanging gold for silver or different currencies).

Key Terms from Source Passage

In Hanafi fiqh, why can a preemptor claim shuf'ah after iqalah?
A) Because iqalah is a rescission
B) Because iqalah is considered a new sale for third parties
C) Because the preemptor's right is perpetual
D) Because the original contract is void

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a rescission between parties but a new sale for third parties, which protects third-party rights like preemption and ensures Shar'i conditions for currency exchange.

Detailed Rulings of Iqalah: Possession, Gift, Destruction, and Barter
Explains detailed scenarios of iqalah: gift, destruction, and barter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules on iqalah in scenarios involving gift after iqalah, destruction of the item or price, partial destruction, and barter exchanges.

Core Idea
Destruction of the sold item prevents iqalah; destruction of the price does not. Gift after iqalah is valid and does not invalidate the rescission.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira continues with specific scenarios that test the boundaries of iqalah. It addresses what happens when the seller gives the item as a gift after iqalah, when the item is destroyed before or after iqalah, and when the contract was a barter of specific items. These rulings stem from the principle that the item is the subject matter of the contract, while the price is not specified (in non-barter cases).

Guided Path
1
Analyze the gift scenario
The text presents a case where after iqalah, the seller gives the item back to the buyer as a gift before taking possession. Identify the ruling: the gift is valid and the iqalah stands. Contrast with if iqalah were a new sale: the buyer's gift to the seller would rescind the sale.
This shows iqalah is not a sale between the parties.
2
Examine the destruction rulings
The text distinguishes between destruction of the price (ثمن) and the sold item (مبيع). Price destruction does not prevent iqalah; item destruction does. The reasoning: the contract exists through the specific item, not the price.
This is based on the rule that the price is not specified (لا يتعين) in the contract.
3
Understand partial destruction
If part of the sold item is destroyed, iqalah is valid for the remainder because the contract still exists for that part.
Example: if a slave loses a hand, iqalah is still possible.
4
Apply to barter (عين بعين)
When both items are specified (e.g., barter of two specific items), iqalah is valid even if one item is destroyed after possession, but the buyer of the destroyed item must compensate with value or like.
This is because both items are specified in the contract.

باب الإقالة
جاز البيع، ولو كانت بيعاً: لكان لا يجوز أن يبيعه منه قبل القبض، ولو باعه من غيره : لا يجوز؛ لأنها في حق غيرهما : بيع جديد.

والرابعة: إذا وهب البائع المبيعَ من المشتري بعد الإقالة قبل القبض والاسترداد: فالهبة جائزة، وصار المبيع للمشتري بالهبة، ولا تبطل الإقالة، فلو كانت بيعاً فوهبه المشتري من البائع، فقبله البائع: ينفسخ البيع، يعني إذا وهب المشتري المبيع قبل القبض للبائع، فقبله البائع: انفسخ البيع بينهما.

وإذا تبايعا عيناً بعين مما يتعين كل واحد منهما بالعقد، وتقابضا، ثم هلك أحدهما في يد مشتريه، ثم تقايلا فالإقالة صحيحة، وعلى مشتري الهالك قيمته، أو مثله إن كان مثلياً، ويسلمه إلى صاحبه، ويسترد العين منه.
وكذا لو تقايلا والمعقود عليهما قائمان، ثم هلك أحدهما.

Chapter of Iqalah: If the sale is permitted, and if it were a sale, then it would not be permissible for him to sell it to him before taking possession, and if he sold it to someone else: it is not permissible; because it is a new sale in respect to other than the two parties. If it were a sale, and the buyer gives it as a gift to the seller, and the seller accepts: the sale is rescinded, meaning if the buyer gives the sold item as a gift to the seller before taking possession, and the seller accepts: the sale is rescinded between them. The fifth: If it was something measured by volume or weight, and he sold it by measure or weight, then they made iqalah, and the seller retrieved the sold item without measuring or weighing: his taking possession is valid. If it were a sale, his taking possession would not be valid without measuring or weighing, rather he would have to repeat them. The pre-emptor. The benefit of his statement: 'a sale in respect to other than the two parties': if the sold item was real estate, and the pre-emptor relinquished his right in the original contract, then they made iqalah, and the sold item returns to the seller's ownership, and he seeks preemption in the iqalah: he has that right; because it is a new sale in respect to other than the two parties. Likewise if the sold item was currency exchange, then taking possession from both sides is a condition for the validity of iqalah, so it is considered like a new sale in Islamic law. Likewise if a person gives a gift, takes possession, and does not compensate until the donee sells it to another, then they make iqalah: the donor has no right to revoke his gift to the seller, and it is as if the seller bought it in respect to the donor. Destruction of the price does not prevent the validity of iqalah. Destruction of the sold item prevents it. If part of the sold item is destroyed: iqalah is valid for the remainder. His statement: (Destruction of the price does not prevent the validity of iqalah, and destruction of the sold item prevents it); because revoking the sale requires its existence, and it exists through the sold item, not the price. His statement: destruction of the sold item prevents it: because if the sold item is destroyed, the price remains, and the price is not specified by the contract. If what is not specified by the contract remains, and what is specified perishes, then there remains no contract, so there is no meaning to revoking it. If they exchange a specific item for a specific item, each of which is specified by the contract, and they take possession, then one of them is destroyed in the hands of its buyer, then they make iqalah: the iqalah is valid, and the buyer of the destroyed item must pay its value or its like if it is fungible, and hand it over to his companion, and retrieve the specific item from him. Likewise if they make iqalah while both contracted items are existing, then one of them is destroyed. His statement: (If part of the sold item is destroyed: iqalah is valid for the remainder) because the sale exists in it. If the sold item was a slave, and his hand is severed while with the buyer, and he takes the compensation for the injury (wound), then they make iqalah: he returns all the price, and takes the slave, and the seller has nothing of the compensation for the hand, and it is permissible (halal) for the buyer, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira explains key rulings on iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale) in the Hanafi school. The author begins by establishing that iqalah is considered a new sale in respect to third parties, meaning that rules that apply to a new sale (like prohibition of selling before taking possession, or preemption rights) also apply to iqalah in relations with outsiders. However, between the original parties, iqalah functions as a dissolution of the original contract. The text then presents several scenarios: first, if the seller sells the item to another after iqalah but before taking possession, it is impermissible because it is like selling before taking possession in a sale. Second, for non-moving property like real estate, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the 'sahibayn') allow selling to another even without possession, but Imam Muhammad reportedly differs. The fourth scenario deals with a gift: if after iqalah the seller gives the item as a gift to the buyer before the seller retrieves possession, the gift is valid and the buyer owns it by gift; the iqalah is not invalidated. If iqalah were a sale, a gift by the buyer to the seller would rescind the sale. The fifth scenario deals with weighed or measured goods: if the parties rescind and the seller takes back the goods without re-measuring, it is valid because it is a rescission, not a new sale. The passage then discusses the practical benefit of this distinction: for third parties like a preemptor (shafi'), iqalah triggers preemption rights because it is a new sale. Similarly, in a currency exchange (sarf), hand-to-hand exchange is required for iqalah's validity, again treating it as a new sale. The text also addresses the effect of destruction: destruction of the price does not prevent iqalah, but destruction of the sold item prevents it because the contract's subject matter is the item, not the price. If part is destroyed, iqalah can still be valid for the remainder. In a barter exchange of two specified items, if one is destroyed after possession but before iqalah, the iqalah is valid and the buyer of the destroyed item must compensate with its value or like. Finally, if a slave's hand is severed and the buyer receives the blood money, then they make iqalah, the buyer returns the full price and takes the slave, and keeps the blood money because it is compensation for injury that occurred during his ownership.

Key Terms
هلاك الثمن (halāk al-thaman): Destruction of the price (money or non-specific payment).
هلاك المبيع (halāk al-mabī'): Destruction of the sold item (the specific object).
عين بعين ('ayn bi 'ayn): Exchange of one specific item for another specific item (barter).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a sold item is partially destroyed, can iqalah still take place?
A) No, because the item is not whole
B) Yes, for the remaining part
C) Only if the buyer agrees
D) No, because partial destruction is like total destruction

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is not possible if the entire sold item is destroyed, but it is possible if only part is destroyed, and destruction of the price does not affect validity.

Rulings on Iqlah: Effect of Destruction of Price or Goods
Explains the effect of destruction of price or goods on iqlah validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqlah when the price, goods, or part of the goods are destroyed, and explain the legal reasoning based on the concept of determination (ta'ayyun).

Core Idea
Iqlah is valid when the price is destroyed but invalid when the goods are destroyed, because the contract exists through the goods, not the price.

This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah explains a key ruling on iqlah (mutual rescission). The Hanafi school treats iqlah as a lifting of the original contract, not a new sale. This distinction has practical consequences when one of the exchanged items is destroyed before the rescission. We will walk through the three scenarios: destruction of the price, destruction of the goods, and partial destruction, each with a rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is iqlah (mutual rescission of a sale). The ruling depends on what is destroyed: price (thaman) or goods (mabi'). If price is destroyed, iqlah is valid; if goods are destroyed, iqlah is invalid; if part of goods is destroyed, iqlah is valid for the remainder.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): iqlah is a lifting of the contract, and lifting requires that the contract still exists. The contract exists through the goods (mabi'), which are determined (mutayyan), not through the price, which is a liability (dayn).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof connect to the ruling? The contract is 'qa'im' (standing) through the goods. If the goods are gone, the contract is effectively non-existent, so there is nothing to lift. The price is not tied to a specific item, so its destruction does not affect the contract's existence.
4
Apply to scenarios
Scenario 1: Price destroyed → iqlah valid. Scenario 2: Goods destroyed → iqlah invalid. Scenario 3: Part of goods destroyed → iqlah valid for the remaining part, because the sale continues for that part.
5
Analyze the special case (barter and compensation)
If two specific items are exchanged (barter), each is determined. If one is destroyed after exchange, iqlah is valid but the buyer of the destroyed item must pay its value or its like to restore the status quo. Similarly, if a slave is injured and compensation taken, iqlah is valid and the buyer keeps the compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإقالة
وهلاك الثمن : لا يمنع صحة الإقالة.
وهلاك المبيع : يمنع منها .
فإن هلك بعض المبيع : جازت الإقالة في باقيه .
قوله: (وهلاك الثمن: لا يمنع صحة الإقالة، وهلاك المبيع: يمنع منها)؛ لأن رفع البيع يستدعي قيامه، وهو قائم بالمبيع، دون الثمن. وقوله : وهلاك المبيع يمنع منها : لأنه إذا هلك المبيع: بقي الثمن، والثمن لا يتعين بالعقد، وإذا بقي ما لا يتعين بالعقد، وهلك ما يتعين بالعقد : لم يبق هناك عقد، فلا معنى لرفعه.
وإذا تبايعا عيناً بعين مما يتعين كل واحد منهما بالعقد، وتقابضا، ثم هلك أحدهما في يد مشتريه، ثم تقايلا فالإقالة صحيحة، وعلى مشتري الهالك قيمته، أو مثله إن كان مثلياً، ويسلمه إلى صاحبه، ويسترد العين منه.
وكذا لو تقايلا والمعقود عليهما قائمان، ثم هلك أحدهما.
قوله: (وإن هلك بعض المبيع: جازت الإقالة في باقيه)؛ لقيام البيع فيه. ولو كان المبيع عبداً، فقُطعت يده عند المشتري، وأخذ أرشها، ثم تقايلا : ردَّ الثمن كلَّه، وأخذ العبد، ولا شيء للبائع من أرش اليد، ويطيب للمشتري، والله تعالى أعلم.
Chapter of Iqlah. Destruction of the price does not prevent validity of iqlah. Destruction of the goods prevents it. If some of the goods is destroyed, iqlah is valid for the remainder. He said: 'Destruction of the price does not prevent validity of iqlah, and destruction of the goods prevents it' because lifting the sale requires its existence, and it exists in the goods, not the price. And his saying: 'Destruction of the goods prevents it' because if the goods are destroyed, the price remains, and the price is not determined by the contract, and if what is not determined by the contract remains, and what is determined by the contract is destroyed, then there is no contract left, so there is no meaning to lifting it. And if two persons exchange a specific item for a specific item, each being determined by the contract, and they exchange possession, then one of them is destroyed in the hand of its buyer, and then they perform iqlah, the iqlah is valid, and on the buyer of the destroyed item is its value, or its like if it is a fungible, and he gives it to his companion, and takes back the specific item. Similarly, if they perform iqlah while the contracted items are both extant, then one of them is destroyed. He said: 'If some of the goods is destroyed, iqlah is valid for the remainder' because the sale remains regarding it. And if the goods was a slave, and his hand was cut off while with the buyer, and he took compensation for it, and then they perform iqlah, he returns all the price, takes the slave, and nothing is owed to the seller from the compensation, and it is permissible for the buyer, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This is a commentary on a ruling from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar concerning iqlah (mutual rescission of sale). The author explains the effects of destruction of either the price or the goods. The ruling: if the price (thaman) is destroyed after the sale but before iqlah, iqlah remains valid because the price is not 'determined' (mutayyan) by the contract—it is a liability in the buyer's dhimmah. The goods (mabi'), however, are determined; the contract's existence hinges on them. If the goods are entirely destroyed, there is no contract to rescind. If only part is destroyed, iqlah is valid for the intact portion, as the sale continues for that part. The author then discusses a special case: two specific items exchanged (like a barter), where each is determined. If one is destroyed, iqlah is valid, and the buyer of the destroyed item must pay its value or its like to restore the status quo. Finally, an example: a slave loses a hand, compensation (arsh) is taken, and then iqlah occurs—the buyer returns the full price, keeps the compensation, and the seller gets the slave with the injury. The reasoning is based on istihsan and the logical necessity of the contract's existence for its rescission.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract by the parties together; it is not a new sale but a reversal.
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale, which is not determined (mutayyan) by the contract unless specified as a specific item; typically a liability (dayn).
مبيع (mabī‘): The goods sold, which are determined (mutayyan) by the contract; the contract's existence depends on it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on iqlah if the entire mabi' (goods) is destroyed before the parties rescind?
A) Iqlah is valid and the buyer must return the price.
B) Iqlah is invalid because there is no contract to rescind.
C) Iqlah is valid only if the parties agree to a new sale.
D) Iqlah is valid and the seller must compensate the buyer.

Key Takeaway
In iqlah, the contract's existence depends on the goods, not the price; thus, destruction of goods invalidates iqlah, but destruction of price does not.
