Definition of Murabahah and Types of Trust Sales
Defines murabahah, classifies types of sales, and begins parsing the definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define murabahah, classify sales into musawamah and dhaman, and identify the subtypes of trust sales.

Core Idea
Murabahah is a trust-based sale where the seller discloses cost plus profit, but it applies only to goods, not currencies.

In this lesson, we begin the chapter on murabahah and tawliyah from Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah. The author first defines murabahah and then categorizes sales into bargaining (musawamah) and trust-based (dhaman) sales. Trust sales include murabahah (cost plus profit), muwada'ah (cost minus discount), and tawliyah (cost without profit or loss). The lesson also begins parsing the definition, highlighting that murabahah is restricted to goods ('urud) and not applicable to ribawi items like currency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Main Types of Sales
The passage states that sales are of two types: musawamah (bargaining) and dhaman (trust). Musawamah is the default type where price is negotiated without cost disclosure. Dhaman relies on the seller's honesty about the cost.
Musawamah is the ordinary sale; dhaman is trust-based.
2
Distinguish the Three Subtypes of Dhaman
Dhaman includes murabahah (cost + profit), muwada'ah (cost - discount), and tawliyah (exact cost). Understand each.
Tawliyah of part becomes a partnership (ishtirak).
3
Analyze the Definition of Murabahah
The definition: 'transfer of what one owns by the first contract for the first price with an increase of profit.' Break down each element: ownership, first contract, first price, profit increase.
The phrase 'by the first contract' indicates the original purchase.
4
Examine the First Objection on the Definition
The commentator notes the phrase 'what one owns' must be qualified as goods ('urud). Because if the item is ribawi (e.g., dinars for dinars), it cannot be sold as murabahah due to sarf rules.
This restriction prevents riba in murabahah transactions.

باب المرابحة والتولية
المرابحةُ : نَقْلُ ما مَلَكَه بالعقد الأول بالثمن الأول مع زيادة ربح .
باب المرابحة والتولية
البيع على ضربين: بيع مساومة، وبيع ضمان.
فبيع المساومة : هو ما تقدم من البياعات.
وبيع الضمان ثلاثةُ :أضرب بيع المرابحة، وبيع المواضعة، وبيع التولية.
والتولية على ضربين : تولية الكل، وتولية البعض:
فتولية الكل: تولية، وتولية البعض : اشتراك.
قال رحمه الله : (المرابحة : نَقْلُ ما مَلَكَه بالعقد الأول بالثمن الأول مع زيادة ربح).
اعلم أن في كل قيد من هذه القيود اعتراضاً:
فقوله : نَقلُ ما ملكه ينبغي أن يُقال: من العروض؛ لأنه إذا اشترى الدنانير بالدنانير ، أو الدراهم بالدراهم : لا يجوز له بيع الدنانير والدراهم مرابحة.
Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. Murabahah is the transfer of what one owns by the first contract for the first price with an increase of profit. Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. Sale is of two types: sale of bargaining (musawamah) and sale of trust (dhaman). Sale of bargaining is what has preceded of sales. Trust sales are of three types: sale of murabahah, sale of muwada'ah, and sale of tawliyah. Tawliyah is of two types: tawliyah of the whole and tawliyah of part. Tawliyah of the whole is tawliyah, and tawliyah of part is partnership (ishtirak). He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Murabahah is the transfer of what one owns by the first contract for the first price with an increase of profit.' Know that in each of these restrictions there is an objection. For his saying: 'transfer of what one owns'—it should be said: of goods ('urud); because if one buys dinars for dinars, or dirhams for dirhams, it is not permissible for him to sell the dinars and dirhams as murabahah.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This is the start of the chapter on murabahah and tawliyah from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The author first defines murabahah: it is selling an item that one has acquired through a prior contract at the original cost plus an agreed profit. He then classifies sales into musawamah (bargaining, where price is set without reference to cost) and dhaman (trust-based sales, where the buyer trusts the seller's disclosure of cost). Dhaman includes murabahah (cost plus profit), muwada'ah (cost minus discount), and tawliyah (cost without profit or loss). Tawliyah is subdivided into tawliyah of the whole (called tawliyah) and tawliyah of part (called ishtirak, partnership). Then the commentator begins parsing the definition's wording. He notes that the phrase 'transfer of what one owns' should be qualified as 'of goods' because the rule does not apply to ribawi items like gold and silver. For example, if someone buys dinars for dinars, he cannot sell them as murabahah because the transaction would fall under sarf (exchange) and must be spot. The passage cuts off before completing the objections.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and sells at cost plus a stated profit.
مساومة (musāwamah): A sale where the price is agreed through bargaining without reference to the seller's cost.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers ownership at the exact original cost without profit or loss.
عروض (‘urūḍ): Goods, commodities; non-monetary items of trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible to sell dinars bought for dinars as murabahah?
A) Because dinars are not owned by the seller.
B) Because murabahah requires a profit increase, which is not allowed in currency exchange.
C) Because the sale of dinars for dinars falls under ribawi exchange (sarf) which must be hand-to-hand without delay.
D) Because the definition of murabahah excludes any goods that are not tangible.

Key Takeaway
Murabahah is a trust sale of goods at cost plus profit, but it cannot be applied to ribawi items.

Refining the Definition of Murabahah and Tawliyah
Definition and commentary on definitions of trust sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definitions of Murabahah and Tawliyah and identify the refinements to each part of the definition.

Core Idea
Murabahah is selling at cost plus profit; Tawliyah at cost; each term requires careful qualification.

This passage begins by classifying sales into bargain and trust types, then defines Murabahah and Tawliyah. The commentator raises three objections to the matn's wording to ensure precision. Understanding these refinements is essential for correctly applying these contracts.

Guided Path
1
Classify the types of sales
Identify the two main categories: bargain sale (musawamah) and trust sale (dhaman). Trust sales include Murabahah, Muwada'ah, and Tawliyah.
Tawliyah of part is partnership.
2
Examine the definition of Murabahah
The matn says: 'transfer of what he owned by the first contract at the first price with profit.' Note each component: ownership, contract, price, profit.
3
Follow the commentator's objections
For each part of the definition, the commentator explains why it needs refinement. For 'what he owned', it must be goods not currency. For 'by the first contract', a prior contract is not always required (usurper case). For 'at the first price', additional expenses can be included.
The commentator uses examples: dinars/dirhams, usurper's slave, washer/dyer fees.
4
Apply to Tawliyah
Tawliyah is defined similarly but without profit. Confirm that the same refinements apply, especially the condition of mithli (discussed later).
The hadith of Abu Bakr supports Tawliyah's permissibility.

باب المرابحة والتولية
البيع على ضربين: بيع مساومة، وبيع ضمان.
فبيع المساومة : هو ما تقدم من البياعات.
وبيع الضمان ثلاثة :أضرب بيع المرابحة، وبيع المواضعة، وبيع التولية.
والتولية على ضربين : تولية الكل، وتولية البعض:
فتولية الكل: تولية، وتولية البعض : اشتراك.
قال رحمه الله : (المرابحة : نَقْلُ ما مَلَكَه بالعقد الأول بالثمن الأول مع زيادة ربح).
اعلم أن في كل قيد من هذه القيود اعتراضاً:
فقوله : نَقلُ ما ملكه ينبغي أن يُقال: من العروض؛ لأنه إذا اشترى الدنانير بالدنانير ، أو الدراهم بالدراهم : لا يجوز له بيع الدنانير والدراهم مرابحة.
والتَّوْليةُ : نَقْلُ ما مَلَكَه بالعقد الأول بالثمن الأول من غير زيادة ربح .
ولا تصح المرابحة، ولا التولية حتى يكون العِوَض مما له مِثْلُ.
وقوله : بالعقد الأول: من حقه أي يُقال: نَقُلُ ما مَلَكَه من السلع بما ملكه ؛ لأنه لا يشترط العقد فيما مَلَكَه، ألا ترى أن من غصب عبداً، وأبق من يد الغاصب، وقضى القاضي عليه بالقيمة، ثم عاد العبد: فللغاصب أن العبد مرابحةً على القيمة التي أداها، ولم يكن هناك عقد.
يبيع
وقوله: بالثمن الأول: من حقه أن يُقال: بما قام عليه؛ لأنه لو ضمّ أجرة القصار والصباغ والطراز : جاز ، وهذا إذا جمع: كان أكثر من الثمن الأول.
قوله: (والتوليةُ: نَقْلُ ما مَلَكَه بالعقد الأول بالثمن الأول من غير زيادة ربح).
لما روي أن أبا بكر رضي الله عنه اشترى بعيرين، فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: ولني أحدهما، فقال: هو لك بغير ثمن، فقال: أما ثمن : فلا (1)
بغیر
قوله: (ولا تصح المرابحة ولا التوليةُ حتى يكون العوض مما له مثل، كالمكيل والموزون)؛ لأنه إذا كان له مثل : قدر المشتري على تسليمه.
(۱) قال في نصب الراية ٣١/٤: غريب. اهـ، وقال ابن حجر في الدراية ١٥٤/٢ : لم أجده.
Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. Sale is of two types: bargain sale (musawamah) and trust sale (dhaman). Bargain sale is what has preceded. Trust sale is three types: Murabahah, Muwada'ah, and Tawliyah. Tawliyah is of two types: Tawliyah of the whole, and Tawliyah of part: Tawliyah of the whole is Tawliyah, and Tawliyah of part is partnership. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Murabahah is the transfer of what he owned by the first contract at the first price with an increase of profit.' Know that in each of these restrictions there is an objection: His statement 'transfer of what he owned' should be said: from goods (`urud); because if he bought dinars with dinars, or dirhams with dirhams, it is not permissible for him to sell the dinars and dirhams as Murabahah. And Tawliyah is the transfer of what he owned by the first contract at the first price without an increase of profit. And Murabahah and Tawliyah are not valid unless the countervalue is from things that have a like (mithl). His statement 'by the first contract' – it should be said: we transfer what he owned of commodities by what he owned; because the contract is not required in what he owned. Do you not see that if someone usurped a slave, and the slave ran away from the usurper's hand, and the judge imposed the value on him, then the slave returned: it is permissible for the usurper to sell the slave as Murabahah on the value he paid, even though there was no contract. [يبيع] His statement 'at the first price' – it should be said: at what it cost him; because if he added the wages of the washer, dyer, and embroiderer, it is permissible, and this when summed up would be more than the first price. His statement: 'Tawliyah is the transfer of what he owned by the first contract at the first price without an increase of profit.' Because it is reported that Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, bought two camels, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to him: 'Transfer one to me.' He said: 'It is for you without price.' He said: 'As for the price, no.' His statement: 'And Murabahah and Tawliyah are not valid unless the countervalue is from things that have a like, such as measured and weighed goods'; because if it has a like, the buyer is able to deliver it.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definitions and conditions of two types of trust-based sales: Murabahah (sale at cost plus profit) and Tawliyah (sale at cost, no profit). The author begins by classifying sales into bargain sale (musawamah) and trust sale (dhaman). Trust sales include Murabahah, Muwada'ah (sale at cost less discount), and Tawliyah. Tawliyah further divides into transferring the whole (which is pure Tawliyah) and transferring part (which results in partnership). The matn defines Murabahah as transferring what one owns by the first contract at the first price plus profit. The commentator raises objections to each part of the definition: (1) 'what he owned' should specify 'from goods' because currencies cannot be sold as Murabahah; (2) 'by the first contract' is not always necessary, as shown by the case of a usurper who pays compensation and then sells the recovered slave as Murabahah on that compensation without a prior purchase contract; (3) 'at the first price' should be understood as 'what it cost him' because added expenses like washing, dyeing, or embroidery can be included, making the selling price higher than the first price. Tawliyah is defined identically but without profit. The commentator cites the hadith of Abu Bakr and the Prophet to support the legitimacy of Tawliyah: the Prophet requested one of two camels as Tawliyah, and Abu Bakr initially offered it for free, but the Prophet insisted on paying the price. Finally, a crucial condition is stated: both Murabahah and Tawliyah are valid only if the commodity is of a type that has a standard like (mithli), such as measured or weighed items; otherwise, the buyer cannot verify the cost accurately, leading to potential disputes.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost price and adds a specified profit.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers ownership at the exact cost price without profit.
المثلي (al-mithlī): A commodity that has a standard like (mithl) by which it can be measured or weighed, ensuring it can be returned or replaced exactly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which of the following is NOT a refinement to the definition of Murabahah?
A) The 'what he owned' should be restricted to goods, not currency.
B) The 'by the first contract' is always required.
C) The 'at the first price' can include additional expenses.
D) The commodity must be mithli.

Key Takeaway
The definition of Murabahah requires careful qualification to ensure valid application.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

The Condition of Mithli and the Proof of Tawliyah
Condition and proof for trust-based sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why both Murabahah and Tawliyah require the countervalue to be mithli, and cite the hadith evidence for Tawliyah.

Core Idea
Trust-based sales require a known cost; only mithli goods enable accurate disclosure and avoid disputes.

After refining the definitions, the author lays down a crucial condition: the commodity must be mithli (measurable or weighable). He also cites a hadith to demonstrate the permissibility of Tawliyah. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the condition and the strength of the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The text states that Murabahah and Tawliyah are not valid unless the countervalue has a like (mithli). Examples: measured or weighed goods.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given is that if the commodity has a like, the buyer is able to deliver it (i.e., verify the cost and possibly return a substitute). This prevents gharar.
The text says: 'because if it has a like, the buyer is able to deliver it.'
3
Examine the hadith proof for Tawliyah
The hadith of Abu Bakr buying two camels and the Prophet requesting one as Tawliyah is cited. The Prophet insisted on paying the price, showing Tawliyah's permissibility.
The hadith is noted as ghareeb (weak) by some scholars, but it is still used as evidence in the Hanafi school.
4
Connect condition to the hadith
The hadith does not explicitly require mithli, but the Hanafi school derived the condition through ijtihad to ensure certainty. The condition is a protective measure.
The condition applies to both Murabahah and Tawliyah.

Definition and Condition of Tawliyah and Murabahah
Defines Tawliyah and states mithli condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Tawliyah and state the condition that the countervalue must be mithli for Murabahah and Tawliyah to be valid.

Core Idea
Murabahah and Tawliyah are valid only if the commodity is fungible (mithli) – otherwise invalid.

This lesson introduces the contract of Tawliyah (sale at cost) and a key condition shared with Murabahah: the commodity must be mithli (fungible). The definition clarifies that Tawliyah is based on the cost paid, not necessarily the original contract. Understanding this condition is essential for structuring permissible cost-based sales in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the definition of Tawliyah
Locate the definition in the text: 'Tawliyah is transferring what he owned by the first contract for the first price without any increase in profit.' Focus on the element of no profit.
The term 'first contract' is interpreted as the cost incurred, not the exact contract.
2
2. Identify the condition for validity
Find the sentence: 'Murabahah and Tawliyah are not valid unless the countervalue is from that which has a like (mithli).' This is the core condition.
Mithli refers to goods that are fungible, like measured or weighed items.
3
3. Understand the rationale
The text explains: 'because if it has a like, the buyer is able to deliver it.' The condition ensures the seller can provide an identical substitute if needed.
This prevents uncertainty (gharar) in the transaction.
4
4. Note the proof cited
The hadith of Abu Bakr and the Prophet's request for Tawliyah is mentioned, but the footnote indicates it is gharib (weak). The ruling is still relied upon from other evidence.
Other evidences (e.g., general permissibility of cost sales) support the ruling.
5
5. Apply the condition to an example
The text gives an example: adding costs of dyer, fuller, etc. is permissible, and the sale can be on the total cost. This shows that 'first price' means cost incurred, and the mithli condition applies to such cost-based sales.
The condition of mithli remains regardless of added costs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المرابحة والتولية والتَّوْليةُ : نَقْلُ ما مَلَكَه بالعقد الأول بالثمن الأول من غير زيادة ربح . ولا تصح المرابحة، ولا التولية حتى يكون العِوَض مما له مِثْلُ. وقوله : بالعقد الأول: من حقه أي يُقال: نَقُلُ ما مَلَكَه من السلع بما ملكه ؛ لأنه لا يشترط العقد فيما مَلَكَه، ألا ترى أن من غصب عبداً، وأبق من يد الغاصب، وقضى القاضي عليه بالقيمة، ثم عاد العبد: فللغاصب أن يبيع العبد مرابحةً على القيمة التي أداها، ولم يكن هناك عقد. وقوله: بالثمن الأول: من حقه أن يُقال: بما قام عليه؛ لأنه لو ضمّ أجرة القصار والصباغ والطراز : جاز ، وهذا إذا جمع: كان أكثر من الثمن الأول. قوله: (والتوليةُ: نَقْلُ ما مَلَكَه بالعقد الأول بالثمن الأول من غير زيادة ربح). لما روي أن أبا بكر رضي الله عنه اشترى بعيرين، فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: ولني أحدهما، فقال: هو لك بغير ثمن، فقال: أما ثمن : فلا (1) قوله: (ولا تصح المرابحة ولا التوليةُ حتى يكون العوض مما له مثل، كالمكيل والموزون)؛ لأنه إذا كان له مثل : قدر المشتري على تسليمه. (1) قال في نصب الراية ٣١/٤: غريب. اهـ، وقال ابن حجر في الدراية ١٥٤/٢ : لم أجده.
Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. Tawliyah is: transferring what he owned by the first contract for the first price without any increase in profit. Murabahah and Tawliyah are not valid unless the countervalue is from that which has a like (mithli). And his statement: 'by the first contract' – it is correct to say: we transfer what he owned of the goods for what he owned it for; because the contract is not stipulated in what he owned. Do you not see that if someone usurped a slave, and the slave ran away from the usurper's hand, and the judge ruled against him for the value, then the slave returned: the usurper may sell the slave as Murabahah on the value he paid, and there was no contract. And his statement: 'by the first price' – it is correct to say: for what it cost him; because if he adds the wage of the fuller, dyer, and embroiderer, it is permissible. And this, when combined, is more than the first price. His statement: (Tawliyah is: transferring what he owned by the first contract for the first price without any increase in profit). Because it is narrated that Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, bought two camels, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to him: 'Give me one of them as Tawliyah.' He said: 'It is for you without price.' He said: 'As for the price, no.' His statement: (Murabahah and Tawliyah are not valid unless the countervalue is from that which has a like, such as measured and weighed items); because if it has a like, the buyer is able to deliver it. (1) It is said in Nasb al-Rayah 4/31: Gharib. And Ibn Hajar said in al-Dirayah 2/154: I did not find it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of Tawliyah (sale at cost) and sets a key condition for both Murabahah and Tawliyah: the commodity must be mithli (fungible, like measured or weighed goods). The commentary explains that the expression 'by the first contract' does not require the original contract to be present; what matters is the cost the seller incurred. An example of a usurped slave illustrates this: if a usurper pays value for a lost slave and the slave returns, he can sell by Murabahah on that value even without a prior contract. The phrase 'by the first price' is interpreted as 'what it cost him,' allowing addition of direct expenses like dyer's wages. The hadith of Abu Bakr is cited as proof for the permissibility of Tawliyah, though the footnote notes it is weak (gharib). Nonetheless, the ruling is established from other evidence.

Key Terms
التولية (al-tawliyah): Selling at the original purchase price without any profit.
المرابحة (al-murabahah): Selling at a stated profit margin on the cost price.
المثل (al-mithl): Fungible goods that can be precisely measured or weighed, ensuring the buyer can deliver the same type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the validity of Murabahah and Tawliyah according to this passage?
A) The commodity must be sold with a profit added.
B) The countervalue (commodity) must be from what has a like (mithli).
C) The buyer must know the original contract details.
D) The seller must state the original purchase price exactly.

Key Takeaway
Tawliyah is a sale at cost, but both Murabahah and Tawliyah require the commodity to be fungible (mithli) for the contract to be valid.

Permissible and Impermissible Additions to Capital in Murabahah
Lists permissible and impermissible additions in Murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the permissible and impermissible additions to capital in a Murabahah sale and apply the requirement to truthfully state the cost.

Core Idea
Only direct value-adding costs may be added to capital; ongoing care and unrelated expenses are excluded.

When selling on Murabahah, the seller must disclose the original cost plus any permissible additions. This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah details exactly which costs may and may not be added, and requires the seller to say 'it cost me' rather than 'I bought it for' to avoid falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act (Murabahah)
Murabahah is a sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit margin. The passage governs what costs can be added to the disclosed capital.
2
Recognize the general rule
Only costs that directly process, transport, or enhance the item's value are permissible. Ongoing care, training, and unrelated expenses are impermissible.
3
Distinguish permissible from impermissible
Work through the list: permissible include wages of fuller, dyer, embroiderer, twister, tailor, washer, broker, and driver. Impermissible include shepherd, teacher, veterinarian, circumciser, guard, reward for runaway, criminal ransom, and storage rent.
Note the nuance: driver of sheep is permissible, but shepherd is not.
4
Understand the exception (hen and eggs)
If the seller incurred expenses on an item that generated income (like a hen that laid eggs), he may add those expenses only if they equal the income from the item. Otherwise, he cannot sell it as Murabahah.
This prevents double-counting and ensures honesty.
5
Apply the phrasing requirement
The seller must say 'qāma 'alayya' (it cost me) rather than 'ishtaraytuhu' (I bought it for) to truthfully include all permissible additions.
Using 'ishtaraytuhu' would be lying if additional costs were added.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز أن يضيف إلى رأس المال أجرة القصار، والصبّاغ، والطَّرَازِ، والفتل، وأجرةَ حَمْل الطعام، ولكن يقولُ : قام على بكذا، ولا يقولُ : اشتريته بكذا . * قوله: (ويجوز أن يُضيف إلى رأس المال أجرة القصار، والصباغ، والطراز، والفتّال، وأجرة حمل الطعام). الفتل: هو ما يصنعونه في أطراف الثياب بحريرٍ أو كتان. - ويجوز أن يضيف أيضاً أجرة الخياط والغسال، والسمسار، وهو الدلال، وأجرة سائق الغنم من مكان إلى مكان. ولا يضيف أجرة راعي الغنم. - ويضم نفقة الرقيق وكسوتهم، وعَلَفَ الحيوان بالمعروف، فإن أسرف فيه : يضم قدر المعروف، دون الزيادة. ولا يضم نفقته على نفسه في سفره، ولا ما أنفق على الرقيق في تعليم عمل، أو في تعليم القرآن ولا أجرة البيطار، والختان، والرابض، وجعل الآبق والفداء في الجناية، وأجرة البيت الذي يحفظ فيه. ولو اشترى دجاجة، فباضت عنده ثلاثين بيضة، فباع البيض بدرهم، ثم أراد أن يبيع الدجاجة مرابحة: إن كان أنفق على الدجاجة مثل ثمن البيض: جاز له أن يُضيف ما أنفق عليها؛ لأنه جَعَلَ ثمن البيض عوضاً عما أنفق، وإن لم يُنفق عليها: لا يجوز بيعها مرابحة. قوله: (ويقول: قام علي بكذا، ولا يقولُ: اشتريته بكذا)؛ لئلا يكون كاذباً.
And it is permissible to add to the capital: the wage of the fuller, the dyer, the embroiderer, the twister, and the carriage fee of food. But he should say: 'It cost me such-and-such,' and should not say: 'I bought it for such-and-such.' * His statement: (And it is permissible to add to the capital the wage of the fuller, the dyer, the embroiderer, the twister, and the carriage fee of food). The twister: it is what they make at the edges of garments with silk or linen. - And it is permissible also to add the wage of the tailor, the washer, the broker (who is the middleman), and the wage of the driver of sheep from place to place. He does not add the wage of the shepherd of sheep. - And he includes the expenses of slaves and their clothing, and fodder for animals according to custom. If he overspends on that, he includes the customary amount, not the extras. He does not include his own travel expenses, nor what he spent on slaves for teaching a craft or teaching the Qur'an, nor the wage of the veterinarian, the circumciser, the one who guards the animal, the reward for returning a runaway slave, the ransom for criminal liability, nor the rent of the house in which the goods are stored. If he bought a hen, and it laid thirty eggs with him, and he sold the eggs for a dirham, then he wants to sell the hen as Murabahah: if he spent on the hen the equivalent of the price of the eggs, it is permissible for him to add what he spent on it; because he makes the price of the eggs as compensation for what he spent. If he did not spend on it, it is not permissible to sell it as Murabahah. His statement: (And he says: 'It cost me such-and-such,' and does not say: 'I bought it for such-and-such') so that he is not lying.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage details what costs may be added to the original purchase price when undertaking a Murabahah sale. The general rule is that direct processing or transport costs that increase the value of the goods can be added, but ongoing maintenance or unrelated expenses cannot. Permissible additions include wages for fulling, dyeing, embroidery, twisting (e.g., silk trims), tailoring, washing, brokerage, and driving livestock. However, the shepherd's ongoing wage is not included. Expenses for slaves' sustenance and animal fodder are added only at customary rates, not excess. The seller cannot add his own travel expenses, costs of teaching slaves, veterinarian fees, circumcision fees, guarding fees, rewards for recovering a runaway, criminal ransom, or storage rent. A hypothetical example of a hen and eggs illustrates that if the seller spent an amount on the hen equivalent to the proceeds from its eggs, he may add that expenditure; otherwise, he cannot sell the hen on Murabahah basis. Finally, the seller must use the phrase 'it cost me' (qama 'alayya) rather than 'I bought it for' (ishtaraytuhu) to avoid misrepresentation.

Key Terms
قام علي (qāma 'alayya): A phrase meaning 'it cost me,' used to indicate the total cost including permissible additions.
أجرة القصار (ujrat al-qaṣṣār): Wage of the fuller (one who cleans and thickens cloth).

Key Takeaway
In Murabahah, only direct value-adding costs may be added to the capital, and the seller must use 'it cost me' to truthfully disclose the total.

Permissible Additions to Cost in Murabahah Sales
Listing permissible expense additions to capital

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which expenses can be added to the capital in a Murabahah sale and distinguish permissible additions from non-permissible ones.

Core Idea
Only direct, measurable expenses that increase the value of the goods may be added to the cost; personal or unrelated expenses are excluded.

In a Murabahah sale, transparency is paramount. The seller must honestly disclose the cost price, but certain legitimate expenses incurred in preparing the goods for sale may be included. This lesson from Al-Quduri's matn specifies exactly which costs are allowable and which are not, along with the critical rule of disclosure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key principle
The text lists specific trades (fuller, dyer, embroiderer, twister) and general categories (carriage of food, tailor, washer, broker). Notice the pattern: these are direct processing or transportation costs that add value to the goods or are necessary for delivery.
2
Distinguish permissible from impermissible
Contrast permitted expenses (e.g., tailor's wages) with prohibited ones (e.g., shepherd's wages, personal travel, teaching a slave a trade). The rationale: if the expense is for preservation, processing, or transport to the buyer, it is allowed; if for personal convenience or unrelated services, it is not.
The text explicitly excludes 'rā'ī al-ghanam' (shepherd) because that is a continuous care expense, not a one-time preparation cost.
3
Apply the disclosure rule
The seller must use the phrase 'qaama 'alayya bi-kadhā' (it cost me such) rather than 'ishtaraytuhu bi-kadhā' (I bought it for such). This avoids falsehood because the total cost may include added expenses beyond the purchase price.
If the seller says 'I bought it for X', it implies X was the sole cost; adding extra expenses would be a lie.
4
Analyze the chicken example
The example illustrates offsetting: if a chicken lays eggs worth a dirham and the seller spends an equal amount on the chicken's upkeep, the egg proceeds compensate the spending, so the added expenses are permissible. If no equivalent spending, the sale cannot be Murabahah because the cost would be artificially inflated.
This shows the principle of matching: added expenses must be genuine and not exceed the benefit derived from the goods.

Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. It is permissible to add to the capital the wage of the fuller, the dyer, the embroiderer, the twister, and the carriage fee of food, but he must say: 'It cost me such amount' and not say: 'I bought it for such amount.' Twisting: that which they make on the edges of garments with silk or linen. - And it is permissible to also add the wage of the tailor, the washer, the broker (which is the middleman), and the wage of the driver of sheep from one place to another. Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on Murabahah and Tawliyah. The author explains permissible additions to the cost price. The matn states that the seller may add expenses like tailoring, dyeing, embroidery, twisting, and carriage of food, but must inform the buyer by saying 'it cost me such amount' not 'I bought it for such amount' to avoid falsehood. The commentary expands: it permits adding wages of tailor, washer, broker, and herder for transport, but not the shepherd's wages. It also allows adding sustenance and clothing of slaves and fodder of animals at customary rates; if extravagant, only the customary amount is added. Expenses not allowed include personal travel expenses, teaching a slave a trade or Quran, veterinary, circumcision, etc. There is a detailed example of a chicken: if it laid eggs worth a dirham and the seller spent on the chicken an amount equal to those eggs, he may add that expenditure because the egg proceeds compensate the spending; if he spent nothing, the sale cannot be Murabahah. Finally, the seller must say 'qaama 'alayya' not 'ishtaraytu' to avoid untruth.

Key Terms
المرابحة (al-murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit.
التولية (al-tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers the goods at cost without profit.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The original cost price of the goods.
القيامة (al-qiyāmah (in context: qaama)): The phrase 'qaama 'alayya' meaning 'it cost me' – used to disclose total cost including added expenses.

Key Takeaway
In Murabahah, only direct processing and transport expenses may be added to the cost, and the seller must always disclose the total using 'qaama 'alayya' to avoid falsehood.

Buyer's Remedies for Fraud in Murabahah and Tawliyah
Ruling on fraud remedies in Murabahah and Tawliyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi remedy for fraud in Murabahah (option to take at full price or rescind) and in Tawliyah (deduction from price), and explain the legal rationale based on contract type.

Core Idea
Fraud in Murabahah gives the buyer a choice; fraud in Tawliyah forces a deduction—because one contract remains valid while the other changes its nature.

In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi school treats fraud in two types of trust-based sales: Murabahah (cost-plus) and Tawliyah (sale at cost). While fraud is a violation of trust, the remedy differs based on whether the contract's subject matter is preserved. The passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah explains Abu Hanifa's ruling and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contracts
Define Murabahah (sale at cost plus profit) and Tawliyah (sale at cost without profit). Understand that both are trust-based, requiring full disclosure of the cost.
Murabahah is a common modern Islamic finance contract.
2
State the remedy for fraud in each contract
For Murabahah: the buyer has the option (khiyar) to either take the goods at the full price (including profit) or rescind the sale. For Tawliyah: the buyer deducts the fraudulent amount from the price.
These are the rulings according to Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze the rationale
Read the commentary: in Murabahah, fraud does not change the contract's nature—it remains a Murabahah even if the profit is incorrect. In Tawliyah, if fraud is not deducted, the contract becomes a Murabahah (since the buyer pays more than cost), which contradicts the parties' intention.
The key difference is whether the contract's subject matter changes.
4
Consider how fraud is established
The commentary notes that fraud can be proven by the seller's admission, by evidence (bayyinah), or by the seller's refusal to take an oath (nukul).
This is procedural; the remedy applies once fraud is established.
5
Apply the sub-rule about dirhams
If goods were bought with good dirhams and the seller accepted bad dirhams, the seller may sell on Murabahah based on the good dirhams. This shows that the cost base is the actual good dirhams, not the bad ones accepted.
This reinforces the principle of truthful disclosure in Murabahah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فإن اطلع المشتري على خيانة في المرابحة : فهو بالخيار عند أبي حنيفة : إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء فَسَخَ.
وإن اطلع على خيانة في التولية : أسقطها المشتري من الثمن .
ولو اشترى سلعة بدراهم جياد، فرضي البائع بأخذ الزيوف عنها: جاز له أن يبيعها مرابحة على الجياد.
* قوله: (وإذا اطلع المشتري على خيانة في المرابحة: فهو بالخيار عند أبي حنيفة: إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء رده)، يعني إذا كان بحال يحتمل الفسخ.
اليمين.
والاطلاع على الخيانة إما بإقرار البائع أو بالبينة، أو بنكوله عن
- وإنما أخذه بجميع الثمن؛ لأن الخيانة في المرابحة لا تخرج العقد عن موضوعه، ولم يرض البائع بخروج المبيع من يده عن ملكه إلا بجملة سماها من الثمن، فلا يخرج بأقل منها.

ولأنه لو لم يَحُطّ الخيانة في التولية : لا تبقى تولية، وفي المرابحة إذا لم تُحَطَّ: تبقى مرابحة وإن كان يتفاوت الربح، فلا يتغير عن موضوعه.
Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. If the buyer becomes aware of fraud in a Murabahah: then he has the option according to Abu Hanifa: if he wishes, he takes it at the full price, and if he wishes, he rescinds. And if he becomes aware of fraud in a Tawliyah: the buyer deducts it from the price. And if he bought goods with good dirhams, and the seller agreed to take bad dirhams for them: it is permissible for him to sell them on Murabahah based on the good dirhams. His saying: (And if the buyer becomes aware of fraud in a Murabahah: then he has the option according to Abu Hanifa: if he wishes, he takes it at the full price, and if he wishes, he returns it), meaning if it is in a state that permits rescission. The oath. And becoming aware of fraud is either by the seller's admission, or by evidence, or by his refusal to take the oath. And he only takes it at the full price because fraud in Murabahah does not remove the contract from its subject matter, and the seller was not pleased to release the sold item from his ownership except for a total sum he named from the price, so it is not released for less than it. His saying: (And if he becomes aware of fraud in a Tawliyah, the buyer deducts it from the price); because fraud in Tawliyah removes the contract from its subject matter; since they entered into a Tawliyah contract, if we negated the fraud, it would be a Murabahah contract, which is contrary to what they intended. And because if he did not deduct the fraud in Tawliyah, it would no longer be Tawliyah, whereas in Murabahah if it is not deducted, it remains Murabahah even if the profit varies, so it does not change from its subject matter.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is the ruling on discovering fraud in Murabahah and Tawliyah sales. Abu Hanifa's position: in Murabahah, the buyer has the option to take the item at the full price (including the profit) or rescind the sale. In Tawliyah, the buyer deducts the amount of fraud from the price. The reason: fraud in Murabahah does not change the nature of the contract (it remains a Murabahah with a different profit), while in Tawliyah, if the fraud is not deducted, the contract becomes a Murabahah, which contradicts the parties' intention. The text also includes a sub-rule: if goods were bought with good dirhams and the seller accepts bad dirhams, the seller may sell on Murabahah based on the good dirhams.

Key Terms
الخيانة (al-khiyanah): Fraud or dishonesty in disclosing the true cost price in a Murabahah or Tawliyah sale.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option given to the buyer in Murabahah to either take the item at full price or rescind upon discovering fraud.

D) He must accept the sale at the disclosed price

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the remedy for fraud in trust-based sales depends on whether the contract is cost-plus (Murabahah) or at cost (Tawliyah): option to take or rescind vs. mandatory deduction.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Dishonesty in Murabahah and Tawliyah
Explaining intra-Hanafi differences on dishonesty in cost-plus and cost-only sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on the remedy for dishonesty in murabahah and tawliyah and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of each view.

Core Idea
In murabahah dishonesty gives an option; in tawliyah it mandates deduction – the contract's nature determines the remedy.

When a buyer discovers that the seller falsely declared the original cost in a murabahah (cost-plus) or tawliyah (cost-only) sale, Hanafi jurists disagree on the appropriate remedy. Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the two contract types, while his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, propose uniform rules. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and its practical implications for buyers and sellers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Understand the problem: the seller dishonestly stated the cost (khianah), and the buyer discovers this after the contract. The question is what legal remedy the buyer has.
The contract itself is valid; the dispute is about the buyer's rights.
2
State Abu Hanifa's differentiation
Abu Hanifa holds that in murabahah the buyer has an option (khiyar): he may take the item at the full price or rescind. In tawliyah, the buyer deducts the dishonest amount from the price.
This is the relied-upon position in the school.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each contract
In murabahah, dishonesty does not change the contract's nature—it remains murabahah—so the seller's consent to the named total prevents forced reduction. In tawliyah, dishonesty alters the contract; if not deducted, it becomes murabahah, contrary to the parties' intent.
The 'subject matter' of the contract is preserved in murabahah but not in tawliyah.
4
Present the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf applies deduction in both contracts by qiyas (analogy) on tawliyah. Muhammad gives the buyer an option in both, reasoning that the seller only consented to the named total and cannot be forced to accept less.
Note the difference in reasoning: qiyas vs. consent-based argument.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Work through the practical example given: if the cost was 9 but seller claimed 10, then sold as tawliyah (cost) or murabahah at 11 (cost+1). Work out each imam's ruling on the amount owed.
Abu Yusuf forces the buyer to pay full price but recovers dishonesty plus profit share in murabahah.

باب المرابحة والتولية فإن اطلع المشتري على خيانة في المرابحة : فهو بالخيار عند أبي حنيفة : إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء فَسَخَ. وإن اطلع على خيانة في التولية : أسقطها المشتري من الثمن . ولو اشترى سلعة بدراهم جياد، فرضي البائع بأخذ الزيوف عنها: جاز له أن يبيعها مرابحة على الجياد. * قوله: (وإذا اطلع المشتري على خيانة في المرابحة: فهو بالخيار عند أبي حنيفة: إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء رده)، يعني إذا كان بحال يحتمل الفسخ. واليمين. والاطلاع على الخيانة إما بإقرار البائع أو بالبينة، أو بنكوله عن اليمين. وإنما أخذه بجميع الثمن؛ لأن الخيانة في المرابحة لا تخرج العقد عن موضوعه، ولم يرض البائع بخروج المبيع من يده عن ملكه إلا بجملة سماها من الثمن، فلا يخرج بأقل منها. قوله: (وإن اطلع على خيانة في التولية أسقطها المشتري من الثمن)؛ لأن الخيانة في التولية تُخرج العقدَ عن موضوعه؛ لأنهما دخلا في عقد التولية، فلو نفينا الخيانة كان عقد مرابحة، وذلك ضد ما قصداه. ولأنه لو لم يَحُطّ الخيانة في التولية : لا تبقى تولية، وفي المرابحة إذا لم تُحَطَّ: تبقى مرابحة وإن كان يتفاوت الربح، فلا يتغير عن موضوعه. وقال أبو يوسف : يَحُطُّ فيهما، وقال محمد : لا يَحُطُّ فيهما . فلو هلك المبيع قبل أن يرده، أو حدث فيه ما يمنع الفسخ: يلزمه الثمن. * قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف : يَحُطُّ فيهما)؛ قياساً على التولية. * قوله: (وقال محمد: لا يَحطُّ فيهما ، وله الخيار)؛ لأنه لم يرض بخروج المبيع من ملكه إلا بجملة سماها ، فلا يخرج بأقل منها، فإن شاء أخذ، وإن شاء ترك. وصورة الخيانة في المرابحة والتولية: أنه إذا اشترى ثوباً بتسعة، وقبضه، ثم قال لآخر: اشتريته بعشرة، فوليتُك بما اشتريته، أو باعه مرابحة عشرة بأحد عشر : قال أبو يوسف فيهما ليس للمشتري خيار، ويلزمه البيع، ولكن يرجع في التولية بالخيانة، وهي درهم، وفي المرابحة بالخيانة وحصتها من الربح، وهي درهم وعشر درهم. وقال محمد فيهما جميعاً: المشتري بالخيار: إن شاء رضي به بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء ردَّه، وهذا إذا كان المعقود عليه محلاً للفسخ، وإلا: بطل خياره، ولزمه جميع الثمن. وأبو حنيفة فرق بينهما، فقال في المرابحة مثل قول محمد، التولية مثل قول أبي يوسف. وبيان الحط في المرابحة : إذا باع ثوباً بعشرة على ربح خمسة، ثم ظهر
Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. If the buyer discovers dishonesty in murabahah: he has an option according to Abu Hanifa: if he wishes, he takes it at the full price, and if he wishes, he cancels. And if he discovers dishonesty in tawliyah: the buyer deducts it from the price. And if he bought goods with good dirhams, and the seller agreed to take the counterfeit ones instead: it is permissible for him to sell them as murabahah on the good ones. * His statement: (And if the buyer discovers dishonesty in murabahah: he has an option according to Abu Hanifa: if he wishes, he takes it at the full price, and if he wishes, he returns it), meaning if it is in a state that permits rescission. And the oath. And discovery of dishonesty is either by the seller's admission or by evidence, or by his refusal to take the oath. And he only takes it at the full price because dishonesty in murabahah does not remove the contract from its subject matter, and the seller did not consent to the sale leaving his ownership except for a total sum named as the price, so it does not leave for less than it. His statement: (And if he discovers dishonesty in tawliyah, the buyer deducts it from the price) because dishonesty in tawliyah removes the contract from its subject matter; because they both entered into a contract of tawliyah, so if we negate the dishonesty, it would be a murabahah contract, and that is contrary to what they intended. And because if he does not deduct the dishonesty in tawliyah, there remains no tawliyah; and in murabahah if it is not deducted, it remains murabahah even if the profit differs, so it does not change from its subject matter. And Abu Yusuf said: he deducts in both, and Muhammad said: he does not deduct in both. So if the sold item perishes before he returns it, or something occurs that prevents rescission, the price is binding on him. * His statement: (And Abu Yusuf said: he deducts in both) by analogy to tawliyah. * His statement: (And Muhammad said: he does not deduct in both, and he has the option) because he did not consent to the sold item leaving his ownership except for a total sum named, so it does not leave for less than it. So if he wishes he takes, and if he wishes he leaves. And the form of dishonesty in murabahah and tawliyah: that if he buys a garment for nine and takes possession, then says to another: I bought it for ten, and I have transferred ownership to you by tawliyah at what I bought it, or he sells it as murabahah for ten at eleven: Abu Yusuf said in both: there is no option for the buyer, and the sale is binding, but he recovers in tawliyah the amount of dishonesty, which is one dirham, and in murabahah the dishonesty and its share of profit, which is one dirham and one-tenth of a dirham. And Muhammad said in both: the buyer has an option: if he wishes, he accepts it at the full price, and if he wishes, he returns it, and this is if the subject matter is a place for rescission, otherwise his option becomes void and the full price is binding. And Abu Hanifa differentiated between them, saying in murabahah like the saying of Muhammad, in tawliyah like the saying of Abu Yusuf. And the explanation of deduction in murabahah: if he sells a garment for ten at a profit of five, then it appears...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the consequences of dishonesty (khianah) in cost disclosure in two special types of sale: murabahah (cost-plus-profit) and tawliyah (cost-only transfer). The matn states Abu Hanifa's position: in murabahah the buyer has the option to take the item at the full stated price or rescind; in tawliyah the buyer deducts the dishonest amount. The commentator explains the reasoning: in murabahah, dishonesty does not change the contract's nature—it remains a murabahah—so the seller's consent to the sale at a particular total price prevents reducing it; the buyer cannot force a lower price but can walk away. In tawliyah, dishonesty strips the contract of its tawliyah character; if not deducted, it becomes murabahah, which is against the parties' intention. Hence, deduction is necessary to preserve tawliyah. Then Abu Yusuf and Muhammad enter: Abu Yusuf applies deduction in both contracts by analogy to tawliyah; Muhammad gives the option in both because the seller only consented to the named total. Abu Hanifa differentiates, combining his own view with elements of the others. A practical example clarifies: buyer buys at 9, falsely claims 10, then sells at cost (tawliyah) or at profit (murabahah). According to Abu Yusuf, in tawliyah buyer recovers 1 dirham (the dishonesty); in murabahah, he recovers 1 dirham plus 1/10 dirham (the profit share). Muhammad gives rescission in both. The passage also notes that if the item is destroyed or altered, the option lapses and full price is due.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a specified profit; the buyer pays the cost plus profit.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers ownership to the buyer at exactly the original cost, without profit.
خيانة (khianah): Dishonesty by the seller in declaring the actual cost to the buyer in a murabahah or tawliyah transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the remedy for a buyer who discovers dishonesty in a tawliyah sale?
A) He may either take the item at the full price or rescind the contract.
B) He deducts the amount of dishonesty from the price.
C) He must accept the sale and can only sue for damages.
D) The contract becomes a murabahah automatically.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the nature of the contract to determine the remedy: murabahah preserves the contract with an option for the buyer; tawliyah mandates deduction to maintain its character.

Reduction (Hatt) in Murabahah and Tawliyah: Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on reduction in murabahah/tawliyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on reduction (hatt) in murabahah and tawliyah and explain the practical difference between automatic reduction and the buyer's option.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf: automatic reduction in both; Muhammad: buyer has the option, no forced reduction.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement between two of Abu Hanifa's foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The dispute centres on whether a deceived buyer in a murabahah or tawliyah sale is entitled to an automatic reduction of the price (hatt) or merely a choice (khiyar) to either accept at the stated price or return the item. The difference has significant consequences for the enforceability of such sales and the allocation of risk.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify that Abu Yusuf holds reduction applies in both murabahah and tawliyah, while Muhammad holds that no reduction applies in either, and the buyer has the option to take or leave the item.
Both are students of Abu Hanifa, and their views are recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Understand the proofs
Abu Yusuf uses qiyas to tawliyah: since reduction applies when the seller deceives in tawliyah, it should similarly apply in murabahah. Muhammad argues that the seller's consent is tied to a specific total price, so he cannot be forced to accept less.
The qiyas is jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Yusuf's view, the price is automatically reduced to the honest cost plus profit. Under Muhammad's view, the price remains as stated, but the buyer may rescind (faskh) or confirm (imda') the sale.
This difference affects the buyer's rights if the item is lost or damaged before the option is exercised.
4
Analyse the consequence of perishing
The text notes that if the item perishes before the buyer returns it, or if a defect arises preventing rescission, the buyer must pay the full price. This applies under Muhammad's view because the option is not exercised; under Abu Yusuf, reduction would occur before perishing.
This highlights a practical risk for the buyer.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text does not give a tarjih. In later Hanafi practice, Muhammad's view is often preferred (e.g., in al-Hidayah and al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya) because it respects the seller's explicit consent.
Check the source for the relied-upon position in your school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو يوسف : يَحُطُّ فيهما، وقال محمد : لا يَحُطُّ فيهما . فلو هلك المبيع قبل أن يرده، أو حدث فيه ما يمنع الفسخ: يلزمه الثمن. * قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف : يَحُطُّ فيهما)؛ قياساً على التولية. * قوله: (وقال محمد: لا يَحطُّ فيهما ، وله الخيار)؛ لأنه لم يرض بخروج المبيع من ملكه إلا بجملة سماها ، فلا يخرج بأقل منها، فإن شاء أخذ، وإن شاء ترك.
Abu Yusuf said: He reduces in both (murabahah and tawliyah). Muhammad said: He does not reduce in both. So if the sold item perishes before he returns it, or something occurs that prevents rescission, he is liable for the price. * His saying: (Abu Yusuf said: He reduces in both) by analogy to tawliyah. * His saying: (Muhammad said: He does not reduce in both, and he has the option) because he was not content for the sold item to leave his ownership except for a total sum that he named, so it does not leave for less than it; so if he wishes he takes, and if he wishes he leaves.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a reduction (hatt) applies in both murabahah and tawliyah transactions when the seller had deceived the buyer about the cost price. Abu Yusuf holds that the seller must reduce the price in both cases, drawing an analogy (qiyas) to the ruling of tawliyah. Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, argues that no reduction is required in either case; instead, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to either accept the item at the stated price or return it. The rationale is that the seller only consented to part with the item for a specific total sum, and he should not be forced to accept less. The text also includes a ruling that if the item perishes before the buyer can exercise his option, or if a defect arises that prevents rescission, then the buyer must pay the full price. This establishes the consequences of the buyer's delay in exercising khiyar.

Key Terms
حط (hatt): Reduction in the sale price to compensate for deception in the stated cost.
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost price and adds a known profit.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers the item at exactly the cost price, without profit.
خيار (khiyar): The buyer's option to either uphold or rescind the contract due to deception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, does reduction (hatt) apply in both murabahah and tawliyah?
A) Yes, in both.
B) No, only in murabahah.
C) No, only in tawliyah.
D) Yes, but only if the item perishes.

Key Takeaway
In cases of deception in trust sales, the Hanafi school has two views: automatic reduction (Abu Yusuf) or buyer's option (Muhammad); later practice often adopts the option.

Khianah in Murabahah and Tawliyah: Three Hanafi Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's remedy for deception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on khiyar for khianah in murabahah/tawliyah and apply each to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Khianah in cost price does not always give an option: Abu Yusuf denies it, Muhammad affirms it, Abu Hanifa splits.

When a seller overstates the cost in a trust-based sale (murabahah or tawliyah), the buyer is deceived. But what remedy does he have? The Hanafi school has three different answers from its three leading imams. This lesson unpacks each view and the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
A buyer purchases a cloth for 9 dirhams, then tells a second buyer 'I bought it for 10' and either gives tawliyah at cost (10) or murabahah at 11 (10+1 profit). The deception (khianah) is 1 dirham.
The same scenario applies to both transaction types.
2
Identify the three positions
Abu Yusuf says in both: no option, sale is binding, but buyer recovers the amount of deception (plus profit share in murabahah). Muhammad says in both: buyer has option to accept at stated price or rescind. Abu Hanifa says: in murabahah same as Muhammad (option), in tawliyah same as Abu Yusuf (no option).
Note the split in Abu Hanifa's view.
3
Compare the remedies
Under Abu Yusuf: automatic price reduction. Under Muhammad: voluntary choice. Under Abu Hanifa: remedy depends on contract type.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the sale can be unwound.
4
Analyze the practical consequence
If the buyer wants to keep the item but pay less, Abu Yusuf's view works. If he wants to return it, Muhammad's view works. Under Abu Hanifa, returning is possible only in murabahah.
This affects contracting strategy.
5
Note the tarjih (if any)
The passage does not state a preferred view. Later Hanafi texts may give tarjih, but the lesson is about understanding the ikhtilaf itself.
In fatwa practice, one would check later authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصورة الخيانة في المرابحة والتولية: أنه إذا اشترى ثوباً بتسعة، وقبضه، ثم قال لآخر: اشتريته بعشرة، فوليتُك بما اشتريته، أو باعه مرابحة عشرة بأحد عشر : قال أبو يوسف فيهما ليس للمشتري خيار، ويلزمه البيع، ولكن يرجع في التولية بالخيانة، وهي درهم، وفي المرابحة بالخيانة وحصتها من الربح، وهي درهم وعشر درهم. وقال محمد فيهما جميعاً: المشتري بالخيار: إن شاء رضي به بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء ردَّه، وهذا إذا كان المعقود عليه محلاً للفسخ، وإلا: بطل خياره، ولزمه جميع الثمن. وأبو حنيفة فرق بينهما، فقال في المرابحة مثل قول محمد، التولية مثل قول أبي يوسف.
The form of deception in murabahah and tawliyah: that if he bought a cloth for nine, and took possession, then said to another: I bought it for ten, so I give you the tawliyah of what I bought, or he sold it murabahah of ten for eleven: Abu Yusuf said in both: the buyer has no option, and the sale is binding, but he recovers in tawliyah the deception, which is one dirham, and in murabahah the deception and its share of the profit, which is one dirham and one-tenth of a dirham. Muhammad said in both: the buyer has the option: if he wishes he is satisfied with it for the whole price, and if he wishes he returns it; this is if the subject of the contract is eligible for rescission; otherwise his option is void and he is liable for the whole price. Abu Hanifa differentiated between them, saying in murabahah like Muhammad's saying, and in tawliyah like Abu Yusuf's saying.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage details the three positions within the Hanafi school regarding the case of khianah (deception) in murabahah and tawliyah transactions. The scenario: a buyer purchases an item for nine dirhams but tells the next buyer that it cost ten. Then he either gives tawliyah (selling at cost) for ten, or murabahah (cost plus profit) by selling at eleven (ten plus one profit). Abu Yusuf holds that the sale is binding without option; the deceived buyer recovers the amount of deception: in tawliyah, one dirham (the difference); in murabahah, the deception plus its share of profit (one dirham and one-tenth). Muhammad gives the buyer the option to either accept at the full stated price or rescind, provided the item is still in a condition to be returned. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: in murabahah he follows Muhammad (option), in tawliyah he follows Abu Yusuf (no option, only price adjustment). This shows the school's nuanced approach.

Key Terms
خيانة (khianah): Deception in stating the cost price in a murabahah or tawliyah sale.
خيار (khiyar): The buyer's option to uphold or rescind the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling when a buyer discovers khianah in a murabahah sale?
A) The buyer has no option, only price adjustment.
B) The buyer has the option to accept or return.
C) The sale is automatically void.
D) The buyer must accept the deception.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers three distinct remedies for khianah in trust-based sales, ranging from mandatory price adjustment to a full option to rescind, depending on the imam and the transaction type.

Calculating Reduction (Hatt) in Murabahah Deception
Calculation method for hatt (reduction) in case of cost misrepresentation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi method of hatt reduction when the seller misstates the cost in a murabahah sale and calculate the corrected sale price.

Core Idea
When a seller lies about cost, the buyer reduces both principal and profit proportionally to remove the deception's effect.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayirah demonstrates the precise arithmetic of reduction (hatt) in murabahah. The Hanafi school ensures the buyer is not penalized by the misrepresentation, yet the sale remains valid with a corrected price. The method removes the effect of deception entirely, preserving justice while maintaining the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed figures
Start with the seller's stated cost and profit. In the example, claimed cost = 10 dirhams, profit = 5 dirhams, so sale price = 15 dirhams.
2
Identify the actual cost
Determine the true cost that the seller paid. Here, actual cost = 8 dirhams.
The buyer discovered this after the sale.
3
Calculate the deception amount
Subtract actual cost from claimed cost: 10 - 8 = 2 dirhams. This is the excess in the principal.
4
Calculate the proportion of deception
Divide deception by claimed cost: 2/10 = 1/5. This fraction is used to reduce the profit.
The same proportion applies to both principal and profit.
5
Apply reduction to both principal and profit
Subtract deception from principal (2 dirhams) and subtract the same proportion of the profit (1/5 × 5 = 1 dirham) from the sale price. New price = 15 - 2 - 1 = 12 dirhams.
The buyer pays 12 dirhams, which is actual cost (8) plus a reduced profit of 4 dirhams (original profit of 5 minus 1).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان الحط في المرابحة : إذا باع ثوباً بعشرة على ربح خمسة، ثم ظهر أنه اشتراه بثمانية، فإنه يحط قدر الخيانة من الأصل، وهو الخُمس، وذلك در همان وما قابله من الربح، وهو درهم، فيأخذ الثوب باثني عشر درهماً.
The explanation of reduction in murabahah: if he sold a cloth for ten with a profit of five, then it appears that he bought it for eight, then he reduces the amount of deception from the principal, which is one-fifth, that is two dirhams, and what corresponds to it from the profit, which is one dirham, so he takes the cloth for twelve dirhams.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage illustrates how reduction (hatt) is calculated when a seller misrepresents the cost price in a murabahah sale. The seller falsely claimed the cost was ten dirhams with a profit of five, so the sale price is fifteen. Later it is discovered the actual cost was eight dirhams. The deception is two dirhams (the difference between claimed cost and actual cost). The reduction is applied to both the principal and the profit: from the principal, two dirhams are subtracted; from the profit (which is five dirhams on the false cost), the proportion corresponding to the deception is removed: since the deception is one-fifth of the false cost (2/10), one-fifth of the profit (1 dirham) is subtracted. Thus the buyer pays 15 - 2 - 1 = 12 dirhams. This ensures the seller does not profit from the deception.

Key Terms
حط (hatt): Reduction of the sale price to remove the effect of deception in the cost disclosure.
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example provided in the source, what is the amount of reduction from the profit?
A) 2 dirhams.
B) 1 dirham.
C) 1/5 dirham.
D) 0.5 dirham.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method of hatt corrects the price proportionally to eliminate the seller's profit from deception while keeping the sale valid.

Disclosure in Murabahah: Purchases from Close Relatives
Intra-Hanafi dispute on murabahah disclosure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether a seller must disclose a purchase from close relatives in a murabahah sale, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
When selling murabahah, purchase from close relatives raises suspicion; Abu Hanifa requires disclosure, but the Sahibayn do not.

This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayrah presents a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school. The issue: if a seller buys an item from a close relative (e.g., parents, children, spouse), must he disclose that fact when selling it as murabahah? Abu Hanifa argues yes due to suspicion of favoritism, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that disclosure is unnecessary. We will examine the reasoning and practical impact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Hanafi positions
Read the passage and note the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and according to the Sahibayn. Abu Hanifa says selling murabahah without disclosure is not permissible; the Sahibayn say it is permissible without disclosure.
Remember: both are authoritative within the Hanafi school.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa bases his view on suspicion (tuhmah) of favoritism. He treats the relative's wealth as like the seller's own and analogizes it to buying from one's slave, where disclosure is required.
Qiyas to the slave case: purchasing from a slave also raises suspicion of unfair pricing.
3
Consider the Sahibayn's position
The text does not give an explicit proof for the Sahibayn, but their view relies on the original permissibility and absence of clear tuhmah. They likely hold that the buyer is not harmed unless actual fraud is proven.
Their default is that the transaction is valid unless evidence shows otherwise.
4
Note the absence of a definitive response
The passage presents the dispute without recording a rebuttal from either side. It is a straightforward report of ikhtilaf.
This is common in later Hanafi texts when the disagreement is well-known.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, if the seller fails to disclose the relationship, the buyer has the right to cancel the sale (khiyar) due to deception. Under the Sahibayn, the sale is fully valid and the buyer has no recourse.
This affects the buyer's rights and the seller's liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى سلعةً ممن تجوز شهادته له من الوالدين، والمولودين، والزوجة: لم يجز له أن يبيعه مرابحةً عند أبي حنيفة حتى يبين؛ لأنه يلحقه تهمةً في ذلك ؛ لأنه قد يجعل مال كل واحد منهما كمال صاحبه، ولأنه يُحابيهم، فصار كالشراء من عبده. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : له ذلك من غير بيان.
And if he bought a commodity from someone whose testimony is acceptable for him from parents, children, and spouse: it is not permissible for him to sell it murabahah according to Abu Hanifa until he discloses; because suspicion attaches to him in that; because he might treat the wealth of each of them as his own wealth, and because he shows favoritism to them, so it becomes like buying from his slave. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: he may do that without disclosure.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where the seller purchased the item from a close relative (parents, children, spouse) whose testimony would be valid for him. Abu Hanifa rules that the seller must disclose this fact when selling murabahah, because there is a suspicion that the purchase price might not reflect the actual market cost due to favoritism or treating the relative's wealth as his own. He analogizes it to buying from one's own slave, where disclosure is required. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, however, hold that disclosure is not necessary, implying that the price is assumed to be genuine unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms
تجوز شهادته (tajuuzu shahadatuhu): Someone whose testimony is legally acceptable for the buyer, i.e., close relatives.
تهمة (tuhmah): Suspicion or doubt about the truthfulness of the stated cost price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why must the seller disclose a purchase from parents when selling murabahah?
A) Because parents are minors.
B) Because of suspicion of favoritism.
C) Because parents cannot testify.
D) Because the purchase is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school is divided on whether purchase from close relatives must be disclosed in murabahah, with the practical consequence being the buyer's right to cancel (khiyar) under Abu Hanifa's view.

Disclosure Requirement in Murabahah Sales from Slaves
Ruling with ijma proof on disclosure in murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling murabahah without disclosure when the original purchase was from a mukatab, mudabbar, or authorized slave, and explain why disclosure is required.

Core Idea
When buying from certain slaves, selling murabahah without full disclosure is prohibited by consensus.

In murabahah sales, honesty about the original price is fundamental. This passage highlights a special case: when the original purchase was from a mukatab, mudabbar, or authorized slave, or when one's own slaves buy from him, the Hanafi school holds that full disclosure is mandatory. The reason is that these relationships create a strong suspicion of price manipulation, so transparency is required to uphold trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a commodity as murabahah (cost-plus profit) without disclosing that the original purchase was from a specific category of slaves. The ruling is that this sale is not permissible (haram) without disclosure.
2
Identify the evidentiary basis
The proof is the consensus (ijma) of the Hanafi imams. The passage says 'And they agreed' (wa ajma'u). This is a strong authority within the school.
Ijma is a primary source of law.
3
Understand the underlying reason (illa)
The reason for the prohibition is the strong suspicion of price manipulation or lack of honest disclosure due to the close relationship between the buyer and the seller. This illa applies to all categories mentioned: mukatab, mudabbar, authorized slave, and when one's own slaves buy from him.
The illa is implicit: the relationship creates doubt about the true price.
4
Apply the ruling to new cases
By analogy, any transaction where there is a significant conflict of interest or close relationship that could compromise transparency would require disclosure in murabahah sales. However, the Hanafi school limits it to these specified categories due to the special nature of slave relationships.
Modern applications may involve closely related parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجمعوا أنه لو اشترى من مكاتبه أو مدبّره، أو عبده المأذون سواء كان عليه دين، أو لا، أو مماليكه اشتروا منه : فإنه لا يبيعه مرابحة حتى يبين.
And they agreed that if he buys from his mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) or his mudabbar (slave promised freedom after death), or his authorized slave, whether the slave has a debt or not, or his slaves buy from him: then he may not sell it murabahah until he discloses.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage states a consensus (ijma') among the Hanafi imams that when a person buys a commodity from certain categories of slaves (mukatab, mudabbar, ma'dhun) or when his own slaves buy from him, he cannot resell it murabahah without disclosing the original purchase details. The reason is that these relationships create a strong suspicion of price manipulation, so full transparency is required.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract with the master to purchase his freedom upon payment of a sum.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the owner's death.
عبد مأذون (abd ma'dhun): A slave given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Selling murabahah without disclosure is prohibited by consensus when the original purchase was from a mukatab, mudabbar, or authorized slave.

Ruling on Murabahah When Buying from Mudarib
Ruling on murabahah price calculation with mudarib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the murabahah price when a mudarib is involved, applying the Hanafi rule of using the lower price and the mudarib's profit share.

Core Idea
In murabahah involving a mudarib, the price is based on the lower cost plus the mudarib's share of profit.

Murabahah is a sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit margin. When the transaction involves a mudarib (investment agent in qirad/mudarabah), the pricing method must account for both the cost price and the mudarib's profit share. This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling on how to calculate the murabahah price in such scenarios, using a concrete example to illustrate the method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
The mudarib (working partner) and the capital owner (rabb al-mal) are the key parties. The mudarib purchases goods with partnership funds.
In the example, the mudarib has 10 dirhams and buys a cloth for 10 dirhams.
2
Identify the two transaction prices
There are two prices: the cost price at which the mudarib bought the goods (10 dirhams) and the price at which the mudarib later sells the goods to the capital owner (15 dirhams).
The lower of these two prices is used as the base for murabahah.
3
Calculate the mudarib's profit share
The mudarib is entitled to a share of the profit based on the agreed profit-sharing ratio. In the example, the ratio is half (50%). The profit from the sale to the capital owner is 5 dirhams (15 - 10), so the mudarib's share is 2.5 dirhams.
This profit share is added to the lower price.
4
Compute the murabahah price
The murabahah price when selling to a third party is the lower of the two prices (10) plus the mudarib's profit share (2.5), giving 12.5 dirhams.
This ensures both parties receive their due entitlements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اشترى من مضاربه، أو اشترى مضاربه منه : فإنه يبيعه مرابحة على أقل الثمنين وحصة المضارب من الربح. نحو أن يكون مع المضارب عشرة دراهم بالنصف، فاشترى بها ثوباً بعشرة، وباعه من رب المال بخمسة عشر : فإنه يبيعه مرابحة باثني عشر ونصف، أي بأقل الثمنين، وهو عشرة ، وحصته من الربح، وذلك درهمان ونصف.
And if he buys from his mudarib (agent in a profit-sharing partnership), or his mudarib buys from him: then he may sell it murabahah on the basis of the lower of the two prices and the mudarib's share of the profit. For example, if the mudarib has ten dirhams with half-profit, so he buys a cloth with it for ten, and sells it to the capital owner for fifteen: then he sells it murabahah for twelve and a half, i.e., based on the lower of the two prices, which is ten, and his share of the profit, which is two and a half dirhams.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage deals with a mudarib (agent in a qirad or profit-sharing partnership) who is transacting with the capital owner (rabb al-mal) or a third party. If the mudarib buys goods with partnership funds and then sells them, the method for calculating the murabahah price when reselling to the capital owner is to use the lower of the two purchase prices (the mudarib's cost and the capital owner's cost) and add the mudarib's entitled share of the profit. In the example, the mudarib had 10 dirhams capital with a 50% profit share. He buys a cloth for 10 and sells it to the capital owner for 15. The profit is 5, of which the mudarib gets half (2.5). So the murabahah price when selling to a third party should be the lower cost (10) plus the profit share (2.5) = 12.5.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The working partner in a qirad (mudarabah) who manages the investment.
حصة الربح (hissat al-ribh): The mudarib's agreed share of the profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example, what is the murabahah price when selling the cloth bought by the mudarib?
A) 10 dirhams.
B) 12.5 dirhams.
C) 15 dirhams.
D) 7.5 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
When selling murabahah and a mudarib is involved, the price must be calculated using the lower of the two transaction prices plus the mudarib's profit share.

Disclosure of Credit Purchase in Murabahah
Ruling on disclosure in murabahah from a credit purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a murabahah seller must disclose if the original purchase was on credit, and explain the rationale based on trust and transparency.

Core Idea
Murabahah requires full disclosure of the original cost, including credit terms.

Murabahah is a sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit. But what if the seller himself bought the goods on credit? The deferred price is usually higher than cash. This lesson examines the ruling from the classical Hanafi text that the seller must inform the buyer of the credit nature of the original purchase, ensuring transparency and preventing deception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Read the passage and identify the act (selling murabahah after a credit purchase) and the ruling (it is not allowed without disclosure).
The ruling is a prohibition: 'ليس له' (he may not).
2
Understand the condition
The condition for permissibility is 'until he discloses' (حتى يبين). What exactly must be disclosed? The fact that the original purchase was on credit (نسيئة).
Disclosure removes the risk of deception.
3
Trace the rationale
Why must the credit nature be disclosed? Because the credit price often differs from cash, and the buyer in murabahah relies on the seller's cost information to judge the profit margin.
The underlying maxim: al-ghish muharram (deception is prohibited).
4
Apply to a modern scenario
Consider a car dealership that buys inventory on credit. When selling a car on murabahah to a customer, the dealer must mention that the original purchase was credit, or else the sale is invalid.
This protects the buyer's right to informed consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى بنسيئة : ليس له أن يبيعه مرابحةً حتى يبين.
And if he buys on credit: he may not sell it murabahah until he discloses (that it was credit).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This is a clear ruling that when a seller purchases goods on credit (nasi'ah), he cannot resell them murabahah without explicitly informing the buyer that the original purchase was deferred payment. The reason is that the deferred price is typically higher than the cash price, and failing to disclose this could lead to deception about the cost basis.

Key Terms
نسيئة (nasi'ah): A sale or purchase where payment is deferred to a future date (credit).
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, always.
B) No, must inform.
C) Yes, if the profit is disclosed.
D) Only if the credit period is short.

Key Takeaway
In murabahah, every detail of the original cost, including whether it was credit, must be disclosed to avoid deception.
